# Codex Brief Review — I-f11-004 (ITER 1 of 5)

```
HARD ITERATION CAP: 5 per document. This is iter N of 5.
- Front-load ALL real findings in iter 1. No drip-feeding across iterations.
- Same quality bar regardless of iteration count.
- "Don't pick bone from egg" — if a finding isn't a real solid blocker, classify it as P3/P2/cosmetic; reserve P0/P1 for real execution risks.
- If iter 5 returns REQUEST_CHANGES, the document is force-APPROVE'd by Claude on remaining-non-P0/P1 findings; do not bank issues for iter 6.
- If you detect "I'm holding back a P1 to surface in the next round" — DON'T. Surface it now. The 5-cap means iter 6 doesn't exist; banked findings die at iter 5.
- Verdict APPROVE iff zero NOVEL P0 AND zero continuing P0 AND zero P1.
```

## Pre-flight

- **Issue:** I-f11-004 — Refusal handling for out-of-scope follow-ups. Out-of-scope follow-up → refusal-with-explanation. Acceptance: adversarial test. LOC estimate 90.
- **Substrate today:** I-f11-001 + I-f11-002 + I-f11-003 shipped. `FollowUpAgent.compose` raises `ValueError` only on blank input. There is no "out-of-scope" detection layer — every follow-up is composed, even if its topic does not match the parent's `template`.
- **Honest framing per CLAUDE.md §9.4:** ship a deterministic substrate that flags out-of-scope follow-ups via a heuristic (template-name keyword overlap with the question) and returns a typed refusal explanation. NOT an LLM-augmented refusal — that is post-MVP.

## Plan

### `src/polaris_graph/followup/refusal.py` (NEW, ~50 LOC)

1. `@dataclass(frozen=True) class RefusalDecision`: fields `is_refused: bool`, `reason: str | None`, `template_keywords: list[str]`, `question_overlap: list[str]`.
2. `def detect_out_of_scope(parent_template: str, follow_up: str, *, min_overlap: int = 1) -> RefusalDecision`:
   - Tokenizes `parent_template` (split on `_`, lowercase) → keywords.
   - Tokenizes `follow_up` (split on whitespace + lowercase, strip punctuation) → words.
   - Computes intersection. If `len(intersection) < min_overlap` AND `parent_template != "general"`, returns `is_refused=True` with reason `"follow-up has zero shared keywords with parent template '{template}'; out-of-scope"`.
   - Otherwise returns `is_refused=False` with overlap details.
3. `def compose_or_refuse(agent: FollowUpAgent, parent: ParentRunContext, follow_up: str) -> ComposedQuery | RefusalDecision`:
   - Calls `detect_out_of_scope(parent.template, follow_up)`.
   - If refused, returns the `RefusalDecision`.
   - Else delegates to `agent.compose(parent, follow_up)`.

### Tests `tests/polaris_graph/followup/test_refusal.py` (NEW, ~80 LOC, 6 tests)

1. `test_refuses_zero_overlap_specific_template` — template `clinical_summary`, follow-up `"Why is the sky blue?"` → refused (no `clinical`/`summary` token in follow-up).
2. `test_accepts_one_keyword_overlap` — template `clinical_summary`, follow-up `"Summarize the secondary outcomes"` → not refused (`summary` shared).
3. `test_general_template_never_refuses` — template `general`, follow-up `"random topic"` → not refused (general explicitly bypasses gate).
4. `test_compose_or_refuse_returns_composed_when_in_scope` — typical in-scope case → returns `ComposedQuery`.
5. `test_compose_or_refuse_returns_refusal_when_out_of_scope` — out-of-scope → returns `RefusalDecision` with `is_refused=True`.
6. `test_adversarial_punctuation_and_case` — follow-up `"What about the SUMMARY?!"` → not refused (punctuation stripped, case lowered).

## Risks for Codex Red-Team

1. **False positive refusals.** A heuristic this simple could refuse valid in-scope follow-ups. Mitigation: refusal threshold is `< 1` (zero overlap). Anyone using a single template-keyword in their follow-up gets through. Acceptable tradeoff for MVP per LOC budget.

2. **Template-name design coupling.** Templates without descriptive `_`-separated keywords (e.g. `t1`) would refuse everything. Mitigation: `general` is explicit bypass. Real templates per repo (`clinical_summary`, etc.) have descriptive names.

3. **§9.4 hygiene.** No `try/except: pass`, no magic numbers (the `min_overlap=1` is a named keyword arg), no `time.sleep`, no TODO.

4. **CHARTER §3 LOC cap.** ~130 LOC net (50 src + 80 test). Under 200.

5. **Adversarial test:** `test_refuses_zero_overlap_specific_template` is the explicit adversarial pass-or-fail named in the issue acceptance.

## Acceptance criteria

1. New `src/polaris_graph/followup/refusal.py` with `detect_out_of_scope` and `compose_or_refuse`.
2. Refusal returns a typed `RefusalDecision` carrying the explanation reason.
3. 6 tests pass (including 1 explicit adversarial).
4. CHARTER §3 LOC cap respected (≤200 net).

**Forced enumeration:** before verdict, write one line per criterion 1-4.
**Completeness check:** list files actually read.

## Output schema

```yaml
verdict: APPROVE | REQUEST_CHANGES
novel_p0: [...]
continuing_p0: [...]
p1: [...]
p2: [...]
convergence_call: continue | accept_remaining
remaining_blockers_for_execution: [...]
```

APPROVE iff zero NOVEL P0 + zero continuing P0 + zero P1.

"""Tests for F11 report-scoped follow-up agent (Phase 3 Task 3.1 substrate)."""

from __future__ import annotations

from polaris_v6.followup.agent import answer_followup
from polaris_v6.schemas.evidence_contract import (
    EvidenceContract,
    SourceSpan,
)


def _parent_with_pool(spans: list[SourceSpan]) -> EvidenceContract:
    return EvidenceContract(
        contract_version="1.0",
        run_id="run_001",
        template="clinical",
        question="Initial question",
        queued_at="2026-05-01T10:00:00Z",
        finished_at="2026-05-01T10:08:00Z",
        pipeline_status="success",
        evidence_pool=spans,
        verified_sentences=[],
        frame_coverage=[],
        contradictions=[],
        cost_usd=0.42,
        generator_model="deepseek-v4-flash",
        verifier_model="gemma-4-31b-it",
        family_segregation_passed=True,
    )


def _span(eid: str, text: str, start: int = 0) -> SourceSpan:
    return SourceSpan(
        evidence_id=eid,
        source_url="https://example.gc.ca",
        source_tier="T1",
        span_start=start,
        span_end=start + len(text),
        span_text=text,
    )


def test_answer_with_overlap_returns_answered():
    parent = _parent_with_pool(
        [
            _span("ev1", "Semaglutide reduces cardiovascular events by 22% in SELECT"),
            _span("ev2", "Major adverse cardiovascular outcomes data from FDA labelling 2025"),
            _span("ev3", "Unrelated content about housing markets in Q3"),
        ]
    )
    result = answer_followup(
        parent=parent, question="What does the data show on cardiovascular events?"
    )
    assert result.status == "answered"
    assert "ev1" in result.used_evidence_ids or "ev2" in result.used_evidence_ids
    assert any(t.startswith("[#ev:") for t in result.provenance_tokens)


def test_answer_out_of_scope_when_no_overlap():
    parent = _parent_with_pool(
        [_span("ev1", "Cardiovascular outcomes in SELECT trial")]
    )
    result = answer_followup(
        parent=parent, question="Tell me about Renaissance painting techniques"
    )
    assert result.status == "out_of_scope"
    assert result.used_evidence_ids == []


def test_answer_evidence_insufficient_for_no_content_tokens():
    parent = _parent_with_pool([_span("ev1", "Some content")])
    # Question has only short stop-style tokens (filtered by len > 2 rule)
    result = answer_followup(parent=parent, question="?! a b c")
    assert result.status == "evidence_insufficient"


def test_answer_caps_to_top_three_evidence():
    parent = _parent_with_pool(
        [
            _span(f"ev{i}", "Cardiovascular outcomes data analysis", start=i * 100)
            for i in range(10)
        ]
    )
    result = answer_followup(
        parent=parent, question="What does cardiovascular outcomes data show?"
    )
    assert result.status == "answered"
    assert len(result.used_evidence_ids) <= 3


def test_provenance_tokens_match_used_evidence():
    parent = _parent_with_pool(
        [
            _span("ev_alpha", "Cardiovascular event reduction observed in trial"),
            _span("ev_beta", "Cardiovascular adverse events tracked"),
        ]
    )
    result = answer_followup(
        parent=parent, question="What about cardiovascular events?"
    )
    for ev_id in result.used_evidence_ids:
        assert any(ev_id in tok for tok in result.provenance_tokens)

 succeeded in 1599ms:
"""F11 follow-up agent substrate (I-f11-001).

Phase-1 standalone agent module per CLAUDE.md §9.4. Production wiring
(graph_v4 calling FollowUpAgent on follow-up questions, UI surface,
scope-gate routing) is follow-up I-f11-001b.
"""
"""Follow-up agent — preserves parent-run context (I-f11-001).

Pure deterministic substrate: no LLM call. LLM-augmented follow-up
disambiguation is follow-up I-f11-002.
"""

from __future__ import annotations

from dataclasses import dataclass, field


@dataclass(frozen=True)
class ParentRunContext:
    """Snapshot of the parent run that a follow-up will inherit from."""

    parent_run_id: str
    template: str
    parent_question: str
    known_evidence_ids: list[str] = field(default_factory=list)
    parent_summary: str | None = None


@dataclass(frozen=True)
class ComposedQuery:
    """A follow-up question composed with parent-run context."""

    effective_question: str
    inherited_template: str
    parent_run_id: str
    inherited_evidence_ids: list[str]


def _dedup_preserve_order(items: list[str]) -> list[str]:
    """Return a fresh list with duplicates removed, preserving first-seen order."""
    seen: set[str] = set()
    out: list[str] = []
    for item in items:
        if item in seen:
            continue
        seen.add(item)
        out.append(item)
    return out


class FollowUpAgent:
    """Compose a follow-up question with parent-run context preserved."""

    def compose(
        self, parent: ParentRunContext, follow_up: str
    ) -> ComposedQuery:
        if not follow_up or not follow_up.strip():
            raise ValueError("follow_up question must be non-blank")
        effective = (
            f"Follow-up to '{parent.parent_question}': {follow_up.strip()}"
        )
        inherited = _dedup_preserve_order(parent.known_evidence_ids)
        return ComposedQuery(
            effective_question=effective,
            inherited_template=parent.template,
            parent_run_id=parent.parent_run_id,
            inherited_evidence_ids=inherited,
        )
"""Evidence Contract inheritance for follow-up runs (I-f11-003).

Pure deterministic functions. NO retrieval / network calls — the parent
run's accepted source pool is reused via deep copy, satisfying the
acceptance "no re-retrieval of parent" guarantee at the module level
(verified by tests).
"""

from __future__ import annotations

from polaris_graph.followup.agent import (
    ComposedQuery,
    FollowUpAgent,
    ParentRunContext,
)
from polaris_v6.schemas.evidence_contract import EvidenceContract, SourceSpan


def inherit_evidence_pool(parent_contract: EvidenceContract) -> list[SourceSpan]:
    """Return a fresh list of the parent run's accepted source spans."""
    return list(parent_contract.evidence_pool)


def compose_with_inheritance(
    agent: FollowUpAgent,
    parent_contract: EvidenceContract,
    follow_up: str,
) -> tuple[ComposedQuery, list[SourceSpan]]:
    """Compose a follow-up question that inherits the parent's evidence pool.

    Returns the ComposedQuery (with evidence_id list inherited from the
    parent's evidence_pool) and a fresh copy of the parent's source spans
    suitable for direct passthrough to the merger.
    """
    parent = ParentRunContext(
        parent_run_id=parent_contract.run_id,
        template=parent_contract.template,
        parent_question=parent_contract.question,
        known_evidence_ids=[s.evidence_id for s in parent_contract.evidence_pool],
        parent_summary=None,
    )
    composed = agent.compose(parent, follow_up)
    inherited = inherit_evidence_pool(parent_contract)
    return composed, inherited

   1: """Follow-up agent — preserves parent-run context (I-f11-001).
   2: 
   3: Pure deterministic substrate: no LLM call. LLM-augmented follow-up
   4: disambiguation is follow-up I-f11-002.
   5: """
   6: 
   7: from __future__ import annotations
   8: 
   9: from dataclasses import dataclass, field
  10: 
  11: 
  12: @dataclass(frozen=True)
  13: class ParentRunContext:
  14:     """Snapshot of the parent run that a follow-up will inherit from."""
  15: 
  16:     parent_run_id: str
  17:     template: str
  18:     parent_question: str
  19:     known_evidence_ids: list[str] = field(default_factory=list)
  20:     parent_summary: str | None = None
  21: 
  22: 
  23: @dataclass(frozen=True)
  24: class ComposedQuery:
  25:     """A follow-up question composed with parent-run context."""
  26: 
  27:     effective_question: str
  28:     inherited_template: str
  29:     parent_run_id: str
  30:     inherited_evidence_ids: list[str]
  31: 
  32: 
  33: def _dedup_preserve_order(items: list[str]) -> list[str]:
  34:     """Return a fresh list with duplicates removed, preserving first-seen order."""
  35:     seen: set[str] = set()
  36:     out: list[str] = []
  37:     for item in items:
  38:         if item in seen:
  39:             continue
  40:         seen.add(item)
  41:         out.append(item)
  42:     return out
  43: 
  44: 
  45: class FollowUpAgent:
  46:     """Compose a follow-up question with parent-run context preserved."""
  47: 
  48:     def compose(
  49:         self, parent: ParentRunContext, follow_up: str
  50:     ) -> ComposedQuery:
  51:         if not follow_up or not follow_up.strip():
  52:             raise ValueError("follow_up question must be non-blank")
  53:         effective = (
  54:             f"Follow-up to '{parent.parent_question}': {follow_up.strip()}"
  55:         )
  56:         inherited = _dedup_preserve_order(parent.known_evidence_ids)
  57:         return ComposedQuery(
  58:             effective_question=effective,
  59:             inherited_template=parent.template,
  60:             parent_run_id=parent.parent_run_id,
  61:             inherited_evidence_ids=inherited,
  62:         )

   1: """Evidence Contract inheritance for follow-up runs (I-f11-003).
   2: 
   3: Pure deterministic functions. NO retrieval / network calls — the parent
   4: run's accepted source pool is reused via deep copy, satisfying the
   5: acceptance "no re-retrieval of parent" guarantee at the module level
   6: (verified by tests).
   7: """
   8: 
   9: from __future__ import annotations
  10: 
  11: from polaris_graph.followup.agent import (
  12:     ComposedQuery,
  13:     FollowUpAgent,
  14:     ParentRunContext,
  15: )
  16: from polaris_v6.schemas.evidence_contract import EvidenceContract, SourceSpan
  17: 
  18: 
  19: def inherit_evidence_pool(parent_contract: EvidenceContract) -> list[SourceSpan]:
  20:     """Return a fresh list of the parent run's accepted source spans."""
  21:     return list(parent_contract.evidence_pool)
  22: 
  23: 
  24: def compose_with_inheritance(
  25:     agent: FollowUpAgent,
  26:     parent_contract: EvidenceContract,
  27:     follow_up: str,
  28: ) -> tuple[ComposedQuery, list[SourceSpan]]:
  29:     """Compose a follow-up question that inherits the parent's evidence pool.
  30: 
  31:     Returns the ComposedQuery (with evidence_id list inherited from the
  32:     parent's evidence_pool) and a fresh copy of the parent's source spans
  33:     suitable for direct passthrough to the merger.
  34:     """
  35:     parent = ParentRunContext(
  36:         parent_run_id=parent_contract.run_id,
  37:         template=parent_contract.template,
  38:         parent_question=parent_contract.question,
  39:         known_evidence_ids=[s.evidence_id for s in parent_contract.evidence_pool],
  40:         parent_summary=None,
  41:     )
  42:     composed = agent.compose(parent, follow_up)
  43:     inherited = inherit_evidence_pool(parent_contract)
  44:     return composed, inherited

   1: """Phase 3 substrate: deterministic stub follow-up agent.
   2: 
   3: The real Phase 3 implementation calls the verifier model with a
   4: prompt that constrains it to the parent run's evidence pool. Phase 0
   5: ships this stub to lock the contract: takes a parent EvidenceContract
   6: + a question, returns a FollowUpAnswer with status determined by simple
   7: keyword overlap. This is enough for the F11 endpoint contract test.
   8: """
   9: 
  10: from __future__ import annotations
  11: 
  12: import re
  13: 
  14: from polaris_v6.followup.schema import FollowUpAnswer
  15: from polaris_v6.schemas.evidence_contract import EvidenceContract
  16: 
  17: _TOKEN_RE = re.compile(r"[a-z0-9]+")
  18: 
  19: 
  20: def _tokens(text: str) -> set[str]:
  21:     return {t for t in _TOKEN_RE.findall(text.lower()) if len(t) > 2}
  22: 
  23: 
  24: def answer_followup(
  25:     *,
  26:     parent: EvidenceContract,
  27:     question: str,
  28: ) -> FollowUpAnswer:
  29:     q_tokens = _tokens(question)
  30:     if not q_tokens:
  31:         return FollowUpAnswer(
  32:             parent_run_id=parent.run_id,
  33:             question=question,
  34:             status="evidence_insufficient",
  35:             rationale="Question contains no content tokens after normalization.",
  36:         )
  37: 
  38:     pool_hits: list[tuple[str, int]] = []
  39:     for span in parent.evidence_pool:
  40:         span_tokens = _tokens(span.span_text)
  41:         overlap = len(q_tokens & span_tokens)
  42:         if overlap > 0:
  43:             pool_hits.append((span.evidence_id, overlap))
  44: 
  45:     if not pool_hits:
  46:         return FollowUpAnswer(
  47:             parent_run_id=parent.run_id,
  48:             question=question,
  49:             status="out_of_scope",
  50:             rationale=(
  51:                 "No tokens in the question overlap with the parent run's "
  52:                 "evidence pool. Start a new run to broaden retrieval."
  53:             ),
  54:         )
  55: 
  56:     pool_hits.sort(key=lambda h: -h[1])
  57:     used_ids = [eid for eid, _ in pool_hits[:3]]
  58: 
  59:     if len(used_ids) < 1:
  60:         return FollowUpAnswer(
  61:             parent_run_id=parent.run_id,
  62:             question=question,
  63:             status="evidence_insufficient",
  64:             rationale="Insufficient supporting evidence after deduplication.",
  65:             used_evidence_ids=[],
  66:         )
  67: 
  68:     spans_by_id = {s.evidence_id: s for s in parent.evidence_pool}
  69:     tokens = []
  70:     for ev_id in used_ids:
  71:         span = spans_by_id[ev_id]
  72:         tokens.append(f"[#ev:{ev_id}:{span.span_start}-{span.span_end}]")
  73: 
  74:     sentences = [
  75:         spans_by_id[eid].span_text + f" {tokens[i]}"
  76:         for i, eid in enumerate(used_ids)
  77:     ]
  78:     answer_text = " ".join(sentences)
  79: 
  80:     return FollowUpAnswer(
  81:         parent_run_id=parent.run_id,
  82:         question=question,
  83:         status="answered",
  84:         answer_text=answer_text,
  85:         used_evidence_ids=used_ids,
  86:         provenance_tokens=tokens,
  87:         rationale=(
  88:             f"Question shares content tokens with {len(pool_hits)} pool members; "
  89:             f"top-{len(used_ids)} used as supporting evidence."
  90:         ),
  91:     )

   2: 
   3: from __future__ import annotations
   4:

  10:     inherit_evidence_pool,
  11: )
  12: from polaris_v6.adapters.evidence_pool_merger import merge_evidence_pool
  13: from polaris_v6.schemas.evidence_contract import EvidenceContract, SourceSpan
  14: 
  15: 
  16: def _span(evidence_id: str, text: str) -> SourceSpan:
  17:     return SourceSpan(
  18:         evidence_id=evidence_id,
  19:         source_url=f"https://example.test/{evidence_id}",
  20:         source_tier="T1",
  21:         span_start=0,
  22:         span_end=len(text),
  23:         span_text=text,
  24:     )
  25: 
  26: 
  27: def _contract(evidence_pool: list[SourceSpan]) -> EvidenceContract:
  28:     return EvidenceContract(
  29:         run_id="run_parent_001",
  30:         template="clinical_summary",
  31:         question="What is the efficacy of drug X?",
  32:         queued_at="2026-05-08T00:00:00Z",
  33:         finished_at="2026-05-08T00:00:30Z",
  34:         pipeline_status="success",
  35:         evidence_pool=evidence_pool,
  36:         verified_sentences=[],
  37:         frame_coverage=[],
  38:         contradictions=[],
  39:         cost_usd=0.0,
  40:         generator_model="g",
  41:         verifier_model="v",
  42:         family_segregation_passed=True,
  43:     )
  44: 
  45: 
  46: def test_inherit_evidence_pool_returns_copy() -> None:
  47:     pool = [_span("ev_a", "alpha"), _span("ev_b", "beta")]
  48:     contract = _contract(pool)
  49:     out = inherit_evidence_pool(contract)
  50:     assert out == pool
  51:     assert out is not contract.evidence_pool
  52:     out.append(_span("ev_c", "gamma"))
  53:     assert len(contract.evidence_pool) == 2
  54: 
  55: 
  56: def test_inherit_evidence_pool_preserves_order() -> None:
  57:     pool = [_span("ev_z", "z"), _span("ev_a", "a"), _span("ev_m", "m")]
  58:     out = inherit_evidence_pool(_contract(pool))
  59:     assert [s.evidence_id for s in out] == ["ev_z", "ev_a", "ev_m"]
  60: 
  61: 
  62: def test_inherit_evidence_pool_empty_pool() -> None:
  63:     out = inherit_evidence_pool(_contract([]))
  64:     assert out == []
  65: 
  66: 
  67: def test_compose_with_inheritance_returns_both() -> None:
  68:     pool = [_span("ev_a", "alpha"), _span("ev_b", "beta")]
  69:     contract = _contract(pool)
  70:     composed, spans = compose_with_inheritance(
  71:         FollowUpAgent(), contract, "What about drug Y?"

  77:     assert "What is the efficacy of drug X?" in composed.effective_question
  78:     assert len(spans) == 2
  79:

  87:

 106: def test_inherited_spans_pass_through_to_merger() -> None:
 107:     """Spans returned by inherit_evidence_pool can be merged unchanged."""
 108:     parent_spans = [
 109:         _span("ev_a", "alpha text"),

   1: # POLARIS Operational Charter v1
   2: 
   3: **Authority:** aldrinor only, GPG/SSH-signed commits to polaris-controls
   4: **Effective:** 2026-05-04
   5: **Pinned in POLARIS:** docs/session_pin.txt → charter_sha256
   6: **This document is the highest-authority operational rulebook. Conflicts resolve in favor of this document.**
   7: 
   8: ---
   9: 
  10: ## The 8 Hard Rules
  11: 
  12: ### 1. Roles
  13: 
  14: - **Claude Code** is architect + reviewer ONLY.
  15: - **Codex CLI** is executor ONLY.
  16: - **User (aldrinor)** is spec owner + merge gate.
  17: - No agent has signing authority on any commit to POLARIS main.
  18: - Out-of-order pipeline execution = blocked by Trusted App server-side.
  19: 
  20: ### 2. Slices
  21: 
  22: Work proceeds in slices. **One slice active at a time.**
  23: 
  24: - Slice spec lives at `polaris-controls/slices/<active_slice>.md`
  25: - Claude reads the active slice spec at session start
  26: - Claude does nothing outside its declared scope
  27: - Slice scope = files explicitly listed; everything else is out of bounds
  28: 
  29: ### 3. PR Size Cap
  30: 
  31: **200 LOC per pull request. Hard ceiling.**
  32: 
  33: - Trusted App rejects PRs exceeding 200 net additions
  34: - Larger work decomposes into multiple slices, not larger PRs
  35: - No "this PR is special" exceptions
  36: 
  37: ### 4. Golden Tests as Fitness Function
  38: 
  39: Every slice has golden-master tests in `polaris-controls/golden/<slice_id>/`.
  40: 
  41: - Tests are immutable to Claude and Codex (admin-only repo, CODEOWNERS-protected)
  42: - Slice is **done** when golden tests pass — then **STOP**
  43: - "Polish," "improve," "while we're at it" beyond passing = forbidden
  44: - Test mutation attempts = HALT condition
  45: 
  46: ### 5. Halt Conditions (mandatory, automatic)
  47: 
  48: Claude halts and surfaces to user when ANY of:
  49: 
  50: - Session pin SHA mismatch (any of 4 pinned files)
  51: - Golden test mutation attempted by any agent
  52: - Control-plane file edit attempted (`.github/`, `.codex/`, `polaris-controls/`, `scripts/autoloop/`)
  53: - More than 2 cycles on the same problem
  54: - Codex review unavailable for >1 hour
  55: - PR exceeds 200 LOC
  56: - Reviewer-fatigue indicators trip (>3 PRs queued for human in 24h per project)
  57: 
  58: ### 6. Archive Discipline
  59: 
  60: Code in `POLARIS/.legacy/` is read-only reference.
  61: 
  62: - Never imported into production paths (CI gate enforces)
  63: - Never modified post-cutover (CI gate enforces)
  64: - New code lives in canonical paths (`src/`, `web/`, `tests/`, `docs/`, `.codex/`, `scripts/`)
  65: - If old code is needed, it is RE-WRITTEN under the active slice, not copied from `.legacy/`
  66: 
  67: ### 7. Visibility (no private channel exists)
  68: 
  69: All Claude↔Codex traffic is logged:
  70: 
  71: - `.codex/loop_log.jsonl` — streaming JSONL, tail-able real-time
  72: - `.codex/slices/<slice_id>/brief.md` — Claude's question to Codex
  73: - `.codex/slices/<slice_id>/verdict.md` — Codex's response
  74: - `.codex/slices/<slice_id>/decision.md` — Claude's decision based on verdict
  75: - `.codex/slices/<slice_id>/diff.patch` — resulting code (if any)
  76: 
  77: If an exchange is not in these files, it didn't legitimately happen.
  78: 
  79: ### 8. Deviation Resolution
  80: 
  81: If any document conflicts with this Charter, this Charter wins.
  82: If the Charter cannot be verified against its pinned SHA, Claude HALTS immediately.
  83: Claude does not proceed on memory or interpretation.
  84: 
  85: ---
  86: 
  87: ## Session-Start Ritual (mechanical, every session, every restart)
  88: 
  89: ```
  90: 1. Read POLARIS/docs/session_pin.txt
  91: 2. Run: gh api repos/aldrinor/polaris-controls/contents/CHARTER.md --jq .sha
  92:    Compare returned blob SHA to pin file's charter_sha256
  93: 3. Same for PLAN.md, active slice spec, golden test manifest
  94: 4. ANY mismatch → emit halt notice to .codex/halt_<utc>.md; surface to user; do not proceed
  95: 5. ALL match → read CHARTER (this doc), PLAN, active slice, golden manifest
  96: 6. State to user: active slice ID + current step within slice + next action
  97: 7. Wait for user direction OR proceed if direction is unambiguous from slice spec
  98: ```
  99: 
 100: The ritual is enforced because the next PR fails Trusted App verification if any of these were skipped. The ritual is also enforced socially: the user reads the `.codex/` logs and sees whether the ritual ran.
 101: 
 102: ---
 103: 
 104: ## Plan Edit Path (the only legitimate way)
 105: 
 106: To change Charter, Plan, Slice spec, or Golden tests:
 107: 
 108: 1. User edits the file in polaris-controls
 109: 2. User commits with GPG/SSH signature
 110: 3. User opens PR in POLARIS updating `docs/session_pin.txt` to the new SHA
 111: 4. Trusted App verifies new SHA matches polaris-controls HEAD
 112: 5. CI passes; user merges the pin update
 113: 6. Next session uses the new pinned versions
 114: 
 115: Claude never participates in plan edits. If Claude disagrees with a plan, Claude writes `.codex/proposal_<utc>.md` arguing the case and surfaces it. User decides.
 116: 
 117: ---

state\codex_consultations\bootstrap_round2_response.md:1308:.\outputs\codex_findings\full_audit_pass_15\codex_stderr.log:4089:outputs\polaris_graph\PG_LB_FS_02_materials.json:6418:      "full_text": "Title: Damage-Associated Molecular Patterns and Their Signaling Pathways in Primary Blast Lung Injury: New Research Progress and Future Directions\n\nURL Source: https://www.mdpi.com/1422-0067/21/17/6303/pdf?version=1598869065\n\nPublished Time: Mon, 31 Aug 2020 10:17:45 GMT\n\nNumber of Pages: 20\n\nMarkdown Content:\nInternational Journal of \n\n## Molecular Sciences Review \n\n# Damage-Associated Molecular Patterns and Their Signaling Pathways in Primary Blast Lung Injury: New Research Progress and Future Directions \n\nNing Li 1,2, †, Chenhao Geng 1,2, †, Shike Hou 1,2 , Haojun Fan 1,2, * and Yanhua Gong 1,2, * \n\n> 1\n\nInstitute of Disaster Medicine, Tianjin University, Tianjin 300072, China; lining620@tju.edu.cn (N.L.); gengchenhao@tju.edu.cn (C.G.); houshike@tju.edu.cn (S.H.)  \n\n> 2\n\nTianjin Key Laboratory of Disaster Medicine Technology, Tianjin 300072, China \n\n* Correspondence: haojunfan86@126.com (H.F.); gongyanhua@tju.edu.cn (Y.G.) \n\n† Ning Li and Chenhao Geng contributed equally to this work. Received: 14 July 2020; Accepted: 20 August 2020; Published: 31 August 2020 \u0001\u0002\u0003\u0001\u0004\u0005\u0006\u0007\b \u0001\n\n> \u0001\u0002\u0003\u0004\u0005\u0006\u0007\n\nAbstract: Primary blast lung injury (PBLI) is a common cause of casualties in wars, terrorist attacks, and explosions. It can exist in the absence of any other outward signs of trauma, and further develop into acute lung injury (ALI) or a more severe acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS). The pathogenesis of PBLI at the cellular and molecular level has not been clear. Damage-associated molecular pattern (DAMP) is a general term for endogenous danger signals released by the body after injury, including intracellular protein molecules (HMGB1, histones, s100s, heat shock proteins, eCIRP, etc.), secretory protein factors (IL-1 β, IL-6, IL-10, TNF-α, VEGF, complements, etc.), purines and pyrimidines and their derived degradation products (nucleic acids, ATP, ADP, UDPG, uric acid, etc.), and extracellular matrix components (hyaluronic acid, fibronectin, heparin sulfate, biglycan, etc.). DAMPs can be detected by multiple receptors including pattern recognition receptors (PRRs). The study of DAMPs and their related signaling pathways, such as the mtDNA-triggered cGAS-YAP pathway, contributes to revealing the molecular mechanism of PBLI, and provides new therapeutic targets for controlling inflammatory diseases and alleviating their symptoms. In this review, we focus on the recent progress of research on DAMPs and their signaling pathways, as well as the potential therapeutic targets and future research directions in PBLI. \n\nKeywords: primary blast lung injury; ALI; DAMPs; PRRs; signaling pathways \n\n1. Introduction \n\nExplosions often occur in modern wars, terrorist attacks, or daily emergencies, and blast injury is a common cause of casualties [ 1, 2]. At the moment of explosion, the explosive materials almost instantaneously convert from solid or liquid into gas. Gas expands rapidly from the explosion point and displaces the surrounding medium to create a blast overpressure (BOP), which transfers a large amount of energy to the surrounding medium to form the blast pressure wave [ 3]. Generally speaking, blast injuries can be divided into 4 categories, including primary (injury from direct e ff ect of blast pressure wave), secondary (fragmentation injury), tertiary (injury caused by bodily displacement or building collapse) and quaternary (includes burn injury and inhalation of toxic substances) [ 3–7]. Furthermore, some research proposed quinary injuries, but the specific content has not reached a consensus [ 3 ,5 , 6, 8]. In these types of injuries, primary blast injury (PBI) is a potentially life threatening, multi-system disease [ 4 ,8]. The previous common view was that blast waves mainly exert force at the air-tissue interfaces within the body. Therefore, organ systems with high air content, such as the auditory systems, pulmonary and gastrointestinal, are most likely a ff ected by PBI [ 3]. Among them,    \n\n> Int. J. Mol. Sci. 2020 ,21 , 6303; doi:10.3390 /ijms21176303 www.mdpi.com /journal /ijms\n\nInt. J. Mol. Sci. 2020 , 21 , 6303 2 of 20 \n\nlungs may be particularly susceptible to blast waves given tissue-density gradients [ 9]. Although recent studies indicated that blast-induced traumatic brain injury (bTBI) may be more common than previously believed [ 4 ,10 ], primary blast lung injury (PBLI) is still worthy of attention. The blast wave-induced acute lung injuries (ALI) are generally closed wounds rather than traditional penetrating wounds, which can be sustained in the absence of any other external signs of thoracic trauma [ 6]. However, it may further develop into acute respiratory distress syndrome (ARDS), a more severe physiological expression of ALI [ 11 ]. Although the current research on the pathophysiological characteristics of PBLI has been relatively clear, the mechanisms at the cellular and molecular levels are still not fully understood. Damage-associated molecular pattern (DAMP) was first proposed by Land in 2003 [ 12 ]. It is the general term for endogenous risk signals released by the organism after injury, which can trigger non-infectious and uncontrolled inflammation in the body [ 13 , 14 ], aggravating the lung tissue damage after an explosion [ 15 ]. This review will focus on DAMPs and their signaling pathways related to PBLI, and explore possible therapeutic targets in future clinical treatment. \n\n2. Overview of Primary Blast Lung Injury \n\n2.1. The Mechanism of Primary Blast Lung Injury \n\nPBLI is mainly caused by the blast wave directly passing through the chest wall, which dissipates kinetic energy in the lung tissue and causes ALI [ 5 ]. In 1950, Schardin described three types of explosive forces that can cause injury: spallation, implosion, and inertia [ 16 ]. The model of normal alveolar capillaries is shown in Figure 1a. Spallation is defined as the displacement and fragmentation of the dense medium into a less dense medium (Figure 1b). Implosion is caused by the sharply reduced alveolar volume due to the high compression of air in the alveoli by overpressure wave, and then the alveoli re-expand rapidly by underpressure wave (Figure 1c). Inertia refers to the shear force produced by shock waves propagating at di ff erent speeds through di ff erent density tissues [ 3, 17 ,18 ]. These three forces work together to cause pulmonary hemorrhage and contusion, and once the air in the alveoli enters the pulmonary circulation, it may cause air embolism, resulting in a series of symptoms such as pulmonary edema, pneumothorax, and interstitial emphysema [ 19 ,20 ]. The description of these three injury mechanisms is based on the blast wave generated by open air explosions. The extent to which these forces actually cause damage is not completely clear [ 3 ]. Furthermore, research shows that the reflection of blast waves in confined spaces will significantly enhance their e ff ects and further aggravate the injury [18]. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 20 20 , 21, x FOR PEER REVIEW 3 of 23                                  \n\n> Figure 1. Damage mechanism of blast lung injury .(a) Normal alveolar capillaries .(b)\n> Schematic diagram of spallation . Alveolar hemorrhage causes the release of cytokines and\n> chemokines .(c) Schematic diagram of implosion . Air in the alveoli enters the pulmonary\n> circulation, causing air embolism.\n> 2.2. Damage mechanism of blast lung injury. ( a) Normal alveolar capillaries. ( b) Schematic diagram of spallation. Alveolar hemorrhage causes the release of cytokines and chemokines. ( c) Schematic diagram of implosion. Air in the alveoli enters the pulmonary circulation, causing air embolism. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 2020 , 21 , 6303 3 of 20 \n\n2.2. Pathophysiological Characteristics and Clinical Diagnosis of Primary Blast Lung Injury \n\nThe blast wave causes the rupture of alveolar capillaries, resulting in the presence of free hemoglobin (Hb) and extravasated blood in the lung tissue, induction of free radical reactions that cause oxidative damage, initiation and augmentation of a pro-inflammatory response [ 5, 21 ]. Furthermore, leucocytes can be demonstrated at the hemorrhagic areas within 3 h after blast exposure, and their levels increase at 24 h or more [ 22 ]. Histological examination reveals obvious perivascular edema and massive alveolar hemorrhage within the first 12h, followed by damage to epithelial cells (at 12–24 h) and endothelial cells (at 24–56 h) [23]. Clinically, patients with PBLI often have dyspnea, shallow and frequent breathing [ 24 ]. In chest imaging, PBLI appears as ground-glass opacity (GGO), consolidation, and bilateral flu ff y infiltrates that resemble a “butterfly” or “bat wing” [ 4 ,25 ]. In addition, alveolar hemorrhage, laceration of lung parenchyma, subcutaneous emphysema, pneumothorax, and hemothorax can also be observed [ 25 ,26 ]. \n\n2.3. The Medical Treatment of Primary Blast Lung Injury \n\nAfter an explosion incident, the emergency personnel should first identify whether there is the risk of PBLI according to the place where the explosion occurred and the distance between the wounded and the center of explosion [ 5 ]. It is imperative to perform pre-hospital first aid, and notify the relevant department sta ff in advance to make relevant preparations, so as to ensure the smooth progress of follow-up treatment [ 4]. The current in-hospital treatment strategies for PBLI mainly include fluid resuscitation, hyperbaric oxygen therapy, mechanical ventilation, etc. [ 27 –29 ], and can be combined with drugs that inhibit inflammatory factors, scavenge oxygen free radicals, relieve smooth muscle spasm, and improve lung ventilation [ 30 ]. These interventions aim to control the development of the disease and maintain the vital signs of patients. In addition, the application of new biotechnology such as mesenchymal stem cells (MSCs) in the treatment of PBLI has become a research hotspot in recent years [ 31 ,32 ]. However, due to insu ffi cient research on the molecular mechanism of PBLI, there is no specific medicine for the treatment of PBLI. Therefore, the study of DAMPs in PBLI is of great significance for exploring potential therapeutic targets and developing targeted treatment methods. \n\n3. DAMPs and Their Sensing Receptors \n\n3.1. Damage-Associated Molecular Pattern \n\nThe immune system defends our body with the mechanism of “self versus non-self” recognition. However, it can be activated not only by exogenous “non-self” substances, but also by endogenous DAMPs [ 33 ]. DAMPs are numerous intracellular molecules with physiological functions, which exist in the nucleus, mitochondria, or cytoplasm [ 34 –36 ]. Under normal physiological conditions, DAMPs cannot be recognized by the immune system, but they are released when cells die or are subjected to stress [ 37 ,38 ]. When released into the extracellular environment, DAMPs can activate innate immune cells, such as polymorphonuclear neutrophils (PMNs), natural killer cells (NKs), macrophages, and dendritic cells (DCs), as well as non-immune cells, such as epithelial cells, endothelial cells, and fibroblasts [ 39 ]. The activation of these cells results in the release of various cytokines and chemokines, which further recruit inflammatory cells and activate adaptive immune responses. In addition, some DAMPs can directly activate adaptive immune cells to generate immune responses. Whereas the local inflammatory response plays an important role in tissue repair and regeneration, excessive or persistent inflammation may also lead to a systemic and uncontrolled inflammatory response inducing remote organ failure [ 40 ]. \n\n3.2. DAMP-Sensing Receptors \n\nDAMPs released by injured organs, tissues, or cells can be detected by multiple receptors including pattern recognition receptors (PRRs) (Figure 2), which can, in turn, activate multiple pathways to regulate the inflammatory response. Moreover, one DAMP may bind to a variety of receptors and participate in di ff erent pathways [40]. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 2020 , 21 , 6303 4 of 20 Int. J. Mol. Sci. 20 20 , 21, x FOR PEER REVIEW 5 of 23           \n\n> Figure 2. Relationship between DAMPs and their sensing receptors and the activation of\n> different types of cells. Relationship between DAMPs and their sensing receptors and the activation of di ff erent types of cells. DAMPs are released from cells which are dead or stressed due to tissue injury, and include intracellular protein molecules, secreted protein factors, purine and pyrimidine molecules, and their derived degradation products (DDPs) and extracellular matrix components. They activate downstream signaling pathways by recognizing their sensing receptors to activate innate immune cells or non-immune cells, then adaptive immune cells, and also directly activate adaptive immune cells to initiate inflammatory response. Di ff erent cellular populations communicate with each other through cytokines and chemokines.\n\n3.2.1. Pattern Recognition Receptors Bound by Damage-Associated Molecular Patterns PRRs are expressed by innate immune cells such as PMNs, NKs, macrophages, and DCs, and mainly include toll-like receptors (TLRs), NOD-like receptors (NLRs), retinoic acid-inducible gene-I (RIG-I)-like receptors (RLRs), C-type lectin receptors (CLRs), and multiple cytoplasmic DNA sensors (CDSs) [ 41 ]. TLRs recognize DAMP molecules and participate in inflammatory response [ 42 ]. NLRs promote the activation of inflammatory bodies [ 43 ]. RLRs are one of the main receptor families for sensing viral RNA [ 44 ]. The main function of CLRs is to induce pro-inflammatory response [ 45 ]. CDSs play essential roles in the development of inflammatory diseases and tumors [46]. 1. Toll-like receptors TLRs are located on the cell surface or inside the cell, and can recognize DAMPs such as nucleic acids, HMGB1, heat shock proteins (HSPs), and S100 proteins. Most TLRs (except TLR3) are recruited by myeloid di ff erentiation protein 88 (MyD88) -dependent IL-1 receptor-associated kinase (IRAK), which then activates nuclear factor-κB (NF-κB) to promote inflammatory response by upregulating the Int. J. Mol. Sci. 2020 , 21 , 6303 5 of 20 \n\nexpression of tumor necrosis factor-α (TNF-α), interleukin 6 (IL-6), and IL-8 [ 40 ,47 ]. TLR3 participates in the inflammatory response through TRIF dependent pathway [42]. 2. NOD-like receptors NLRs are PRRs located in the cytoplasm, which can recruit pro-caspase-1 to start the assembly of inflammatory bodies after being activated by DAMPs such as ATP and oxidized mitochondrial DNA (ox-mtDNA), thereby inducing apoptosis and secretion of IL-1 β, IL-18 and other cytokines through the activation of caspase-1 [43,48]. 3. Retinoic acid-inducible gene-I (RIG-I)-like receptors The RLR family includes RIG-I, MDA5, and LGP2, which can distinguish between exogenous RNA and endogenous RNA to resist pathogens [ 44 ]. It can sense DAMPs such as endogenous 5 ′ppp RNA and unedited long self-dsRNA in vivo , and promote the production of type I interferon (IFN-I) and other cytokines and chemokines [49,50]. 4. C-type lectin receptors Some members of the CLR family, such as macrophage-inducible C-type lectin (MINCLE) and dendritic cell natural killer lectin group receptor 1 (DNGR1), are related to the development of inflammation diseases after binding to DAMPs. DNGR1 is a DC receptor which can be activated by F-actin to downregulate the production of the anti-inflammatory cytokine IL-10 in DCs and aggravate the inflammatory response [ 51 ]. MINCLE binds to SAP130 protein to induce pro-inflammatory response [45]. 5. Cytoplasmic DNA sensors Cyclic GMP–AMP synthase (cGAS) and absent in melanoma 2 (AIM2) are two important DNA sensors in the cytoplasm, which play essential roles in the development of inflammatory diseases and tumors [ 52 ,53 ]. Some studies have shown that self-DNA, produced by immunogenic cell death or nuclear DNA damage, can activate AIM2 or cGAS and promote the production of IFN-I and other cytokines and chemokines [46]. 3.2.2. Other Receptors /Channels Bound by DAMPs In addition to PRRs, DAMPs can activate receptors including triggering receptors expressed on myeloid cells (TREMs), receptor for advanced glycation end products (RAGE), several G-protein -coupled receptors (GPCRs), and ion channels [ 41 ]. TREM activation causes cytokine secretion, leading to inflammation response. The interaction between 35 kDa transmembrane receptor RAGE and its ligands also leads to the activation of pro-inflammatory genes [ 54 ,55 ]. GPCRs are known as seven-(pass)-transmembrane receptors and involved in many diseases via the cAMP or the phosphatidylinositol signal pathway [ 56 ]. Ion channels trigger the production of NLRP3 inflammasome and participate in the inflammatory response [57]. 1. Triggering receptors expressed on myeloid cells TREM1 and TREM2 are cell surface innate immune receptors which act as members of the immunoglobulin variable (IgV) domain receptor superfamily [ 58 ]. TREM1 is expressed on myeloid cells, epithelial cells, endothelial cells, and fibroblasts, and interacts with DAMPs such as HMGB1, HSP70, and actin to promote the secretion of pro-inflammatory cytokines and chemokines [ 59 , 60 ]. TREM2 is highly expressed in myeloid cells such as DCs, monocytes, and macrophages, and binds to DAMPs such as HSP60, apolipoproteins (APOJ and APOE), and low-density lipoprotein (LDL) to regulate cell di ff erentiation, phagocytosis, chemotaxis, and other processes, and participate in inflammatory diseases [61,62]. Int. J. Mol. Sci. 2020 , 21 , 6303 6 of 20 \n\n2. Receptor for advanced glycation end products RAGE is a transmembrane protein that belongs to the immunoglobulin receptor superfamily and is expressed by multiple cell types, including neutrophils, monocytes, smooth muscle cells, endothelial cells, and cancer cells, and mediates cell response to DAMPs such as HMGB1, S100 proteins, advanced glycation end products (AGEs), and DNA to promote the expression of pro-inflammatory genes, as well as cell migration, proliferation, and apoptosis [63]. 3. G-protein-coupled receptors Various DAMPs can also promote inflammation via GPCRs. The N-formyl peptide receptors (FPRs) can recognize endogenous N-formylated peptides and promote the chemotaxis of neutrophils and monocytes /macrophages [ 64 ]. P2Y receptors (P2YRs) can sense extracellular nucleotides and promote the migration and activation of multifarious immune cells [ 65 ]. Calcium-sensing receptors (CaSRs) and G-protein-coupled receptor family C group 6 member A (GPRC6A) can recognize extracellular Ca 2+,promote monocyte /macrophage recruitment and the activation of the NLRP3 inflammasome [66]. 4. Ion channels Transient receptor potential (TRP) channels and P2X receptors are two types of ligand-gated ion channels, which can promote the production of cytokines and chemokines and the activation of NLRP3 inflammasome by recognizing mitochondria-derived reactive oxygen species (ROS) and ATP, respectively, and participate in the inflammatory response [57,67]. \n\n3.3. Crosstalk Between DAMP and Their Sensing Receptors \n\nIn summary, DAMPs can bind to related receptors or channel proteins and activate downstream signaling pathways, leading to large-scale cytokine release, including IL-1, IL-6, IL-8, IL-12, TNF-α, and type I /\n\ntype II IFN. These mediators enhance the activation, maturation, proliferation, and recruitment of immune cells at the injured regions, leading to the indirect activation of innate and adaptive immune cells (such as DCs or T cells) (Figure 2). \n\n4. DAMPs and Their Signaling Pathways Related to PBLI \n\n4.1. Intracellular Protein Molecules \n\n4.1.1. Nuclear Protein HMGB1 HMGB1 is a late inflammatory mediator associated with sepsis, malignancy, and immune disease, and is one of the most intensively studied DAMPs. Levels of HMGB1 may reflect the severity of inflammation and tissue damage, indicating a potential role for HMGB1 as a biomarker of ALI, and a potential target for blocking inflammatory pathways [ 68 ]. Under normal physiological conditions, HMGB1 is located in the nucleus, but it is passively released from necrotic cells after PBLI. HMGB1 triggers signaling cascades by combining with RAGE, TLR2, and TLR4, thereby inducing the activation of the NF-κB pathway and participating in the inflammatory response [ 14 ](Figure 3). Li et al. [ 69 ] demonstrated that HMGB1 participates in ALI by activating double-stranded RNA-dependent protein kinase (PKR) in macrophages and inducing macrophage (M1) polarization via TLR2- and TLR4-mediated NF-κB signaling pathways in a mouse model of ALI induced by the bacterial endotoxin lipopolysaccharide (LPS). In addition, HMGB1 levels are also related to processes such as hyperfibrinolysis and complement activation and participate in systemic inflammatory responses [ 70 ]. Int. J. Mol. state\codex_consultations\bootstrap_round2_response.md:1450:.\outputs\codex_findings\v30_user_wishlist_plan\codex_stdout.log:18638:outputs\polaris_graph\PG_TEST_090.json:18078:  "draft_report": "# What are the proven health benefits and risks of intermittent fasting based on clinical research and meta-analyses?\n\n## Abstract\n\nThis systematic review synthesizes 38 clinical trials and meta-analyses to evaluate the proven health benefits and risks of intermittent fasting (IF). The review examines diverse IF protocols\u2014including time-restricted feeding, alternate-day fasting (ADF), and the 5:2 diet\u2014across outcomes of weight loss, cardiometabolic and glycemic control, neurological protection, comparative effectiveness against continuous energy restriction (CER), synergistic effects with exercise, and safety profiles. Quantitative findings demonstrate that ADF yields the most rapid weight reduction, averaging 0.75 kg per week [18], compared to 0.25 kg per week for the 5:2 regimen [29]. Cardiometabolic improvements are consistently observed, though they remain broadly comparable to conventional CER. Emerging clinical evidence also suggests neuroprotective benefits against ischemia and Alzheimer's pathology. While the general safety profile is favorable, with most adverse events classified as mild and transient [7], IF poses disproportionate risks in marginal-weight and vulnerable populations. Major limitations of the current evidence base include high methodological heterogeneity, short intervention durations, and a lack of standardized protocols, which collectively constrain confidence in long-term clinical recommendations. Future research must prioritize rigorously controlled, extended-duration trials to resolve these uncertainties.\n\n## Definitions and Physiological Mechanisms of Intermittent Fasting\n\nIntermittent fasting encompasses a heterogeneous family of dietary protocols unified by the deliberate temporal restriction of caloric intake, yet differentiated by the duration, frequency, and severity of fasting periods. Time-restricted feeding (TRF), a subtype of intermittent fasting focused on limiting the daily eating window without limiting the amount of food consumed [5], represents one end of the spectrum\u2014compressing nourishment into a defined interval, typically 4\u201310 hours, while abstaining for the remainder of the 24-hour cycle. At the opposing end, alternate-day fasting and extended fasting protocols impose caloric deprivation spanning 24 hours or longer, often permitting only water. One investigated regimen, for instance, prescribed water-only 24-hour fasting performed twice weekly for an initial 4 weeks, transitioning to once weekly for 22 subsequent weeks, in adults aged 21\u201370 (N=103) [8]. Between these poles lie 5:2 protocols\u2014normally allowing 500\u2013600 kcal on two non-consecutive fasting days per week\u2014and periodic fasting sequences lasting 48\u2013120 hours. This protocol heterogeneity complicates direct cross-study comparison, as the physiological state achieved during a 16-hour overnight fast differs fundamentally from that sustained across a 24-hour water-only fast.\n\nThe physiological mechanisms through which intermittent fasting exerts its effects center on the metabolic transition from glucose-based to lipid-based energy provision. The majority of health benefits from intermittent fasting originate from the fasting period, which permits the body to predominantly use stored fatty acids instead of macronutrients being consumed [4]. This metabolic switching\u2014the shift from glycogenolysis (the enzymatic breakdown of stored glycogen into glucose) to lipolysis (the hydrolytic cleavage of triglycerides into free fatty acids and glycerol)\u2014represents the foundational mechanism. During the fed state, hepatic glycogen reserves supply circulating glucose; as fasting duration extends beyond approximately 12 hours and glycogen stores deplete, adipose tissue releases free fatty acids, which the liver converts into ketone bodies (acetoacetate, \u03b2-hydroxybutyrate, and acetone). These ketone bodies serve as alternative oxidative substrates for the brain, heart, and skeletal muscle. The causal chain proceeds: fasting duration triggers glycogen depletion \u2192 lipolysis activation and ketogenesis \u2192 hepatic fatty acid oxidation and ketone body production \u2192 systemic shift in substrate utilization from glucose to fatty acids and ketones \u2192 downstream clinical outcomes including weight reduction and cardiometabolic improvement. Intermittent and periodic fasting have potential to prevent and treat disease [3], though this potential must be interpreted with acknowledgment that the mechanistic evidence base is stronger in animal models than in human clinical populations.\n\nThe quantified clinical outcomes associated with these mechanisms provide partial confirmation of physiological efficacy. Intermittent fasting for 4\u201324 weeks induces body weight reductions of 4% to 10% in overweight individuals [1], a range consistent with the caloric deficit and lipid catabolism expected during sustained fasting periods. Meta-analytic evidence from 10 randomized controlled trials involving 701 individuals demonstrated that intermittent fasting significantly reduced fasting blood sugar (SMD = \u22120.51; 95% CI: \u22120.81, \u22120.20; p = 0.001) [6], indicating a moderate effect on glycemic regulation. The same meta-analysis reported that intermittent fasting significantly reduced LDL cholesterol levels (SMD = \u22120.34; 95% CI: \u22120.53, \u22120.14; p = 0.001) [6], suggesting lipid metabolism modulation extends beyond simple fat mass reduction. The glycemic finding (SMD = \u22120.51) [6] aligns mechanistically with the fasting-induced reduction in hepatic glucose output and improvement in insulin sensitivity, while the LDL-C reduction (SMD = \u22120.34) [6] may reflect enhanced lipid catabolism during fasting periods as described by the fatty acid oxidation pathway [4]. However, the confidence intervals surrounding these effect sizes\u2014the fasting blood sugar SMD ranges from \u22120.81 to \u22120.20 [6]\u2014indicate meaningful variability, possibly reflecting heterogeneity in fasting protocols, population characteristics, or adherence levels across the constituent trials.\n\nComparing protocols, TRF and extended fasting regimens operate through the same core mechanism of lipid catabolism but differ in the depth and duration of metabolic switching achieved. TRF, by limiting the eating window without restricting caloric volume [5], may produce a milder, daily ketotic state relative to the water-only 24-hour fasting protocol that required twice-weekly full-day abstention [8]. The 4\u201310% weight reduction observed across 4\u201324 weeks of intermittent fasting [1] likely encompasses both TRF and more intensive protocols; whether the magnitude of lipid catabolism\u2014and consequently the clinical effect\u2014scales directly with fasting duration remains incompletely characterized. The meta-analytic findings encompassing 701 individuals across heterogeneous protocols [6] provide aggregate effect estimates but mask protocol-specific mechanistic intensity. A 16-hour TRF window and a 24-hour water-only fast both activate lipolysis [4], yet the ketone body concentrations, duration of ketosis, and downstream signaling effects (including autophagy\u2014the cellular process of self-digestion removing damaged organelles and proteins) presumably differ substantially.\n\nThe translation of mechanistic plausibility to clinical proof encounters several limitations. First, while the lipid catabolism pathway is well-established in human biochemistry, the specific downstream effects of intermittent fasting on disease prevention and treatment [3] require substantiation through long-term clinical trials\u2014precisely the evidence currently lacking. Further high-quality studies with long-term follow-ups are needed to confirm findings on intermittent fasting in gynecological and breast cancer patients [7], illustrating that even in populations where hormonal and metabolic interactions would predict strong mechanistic effects, clinical confirmation remains incomplete. Second, the metabolic benefits observed in overweight populations\u2014where excess adiposity provides abundant substrate for lipolysis\u2014may not translate to marginal-weight individuals. Intermittent fasting could cause excessive weight loss in marginal-weight individuals, which can affect bones, the immune system, and energy level [2]. This risk profile emerges from the same lipid catabolism mechanism that confers benefit: when fat stores are insufficient, prolonged fasting may mobilize lean mass and impair immunological and skeletal homeostasis. The 4\u201310% weight reduction in overweight individuals [1] represents a therapeutic effect in that population but would constitute an adverse outcome in underweight or marginally nourished groups [2]. Third, the 701-participant meta-analysis [6], while providing the most robust quantitative evidence for glycemic and lipid effects, drew from studies of varying duration and protocol design, limiting mechanistic specificity.\n\nSeveral critical gaps remain in understanding the mechanisms of intermittent fasting. The relative contributions of caloric restriction versus temporal restriction\u2014independent mechanisms or synergistic\u2014have not been fully disentangled. TRF, by definition, does not limit food volume [5], yet in practice, compressed eating windows often produce incidental caloric deficits, making it difficult to isolate the effects of the fasting period itself. Whether the metabolic switching to fatty acid oxidation [4] produces unique signaling effects beyond those attributable to reduced caloric intake represents a fundamental unanswered question. Additionally, the dose-response relationship between fasting duration and physiological effect requires mapping: the twice-weekly to once-weekly 24-hour fasting schedule [8] provides one data point, but the optimal frequency, duration, and intensity for specific clinical outcomes remain undefined. Long-term adherence and safety data, particularly beyond the 24-week maximum observed in weight reduction studies [1], are absent, and the generalizability of findings from overweight populations [1][6] to other demographic and clinical groups remains untested [7]. Future research must prioritize mechanistic trials with precise metabolic phenotyping, protocol-specific randomization, and extended follow-up to differentiate the independent and combined effects of temporal restriction, caloric deficit, and lipid catabolism.\n\n***\n\n### Key Findings\n\n- Health benefits of intermittent fasting originate from the fasting period, during which the body shifts to predominantly utilizing stored fatty acids rather than consumed macronutrients [4], activating lipolysis and ketogenesis as the primary metabolic pathway.\n- Intermittent fasting (4\u201324 weeks) induces body weight reductions of 4% to 10% in overweight individuals [1], consistent with sustained lipid catabolism during fasting periods.\n- Meta-analytic evidence (10 RCTs, N=701) demonstrates significant reductions in fasting blood sugar (SMD = \u22120.51; 95% CI: \u22120.81, \u22120.20; p = 0.001) and LDL cholesterol (SMD = \u22120.34; 95% CI: \u22120.53, \u22120.14; p = 0.001) [6], supporting glycemic and lipid-modulating mechanisms.\n- Protocol definitions vary substantially: time-restricted feeding limits the eating window without restricting caloric volume [5], while more intensive regimens involve water-only 24-hour fasting performed twice weekly for 4 weeks then once weekly for 22 weeks [8].\n- Intermittent fasting poses risks of excessive weight loss in marginal-weight individuals, potentially compromising bone density, immune function, and energy levels [2], and long-term clinical confirmation is needed, particularly in disease-specific populations such as gynecological and breast cancer patients [7].\n\n| Protocol Type | Fasting Duration | Caloric Restriction | Eating Window | Example Regimen |\n|---|---|---|---|---|\n| Time-Restricted Feeding | 14\u201320 hours daily | Not mandated [5] | 4\u201310 hours | 16:8 (16h fast, 8h feed) |\n| Alternate-Day / Extended Fasting | 24 hours | Complete (water-only) [8] | None on fast days | 24h water-only fast 2\u00d7/week for 4 weeks, then 1\u00d7/week for 22 weeks [8] |\n\n## Weight Loss and Body Composition Outcomes\n\nAcross intermittent fasting protocols, body weight reductions are consistently observed, though the magnitude varies substantially by regimen type and duration. Alternate-day fasting (ADF) produces the most rapid weight loss, averaging 0.75 kg per week, compared with 0.25 kg per week for the 5:2 protocol [1]. This threefold difference in weekly reduction rate suggests that the frequency and severity of caloric restriction days directly modulate the pace of weight loss. Time-restricted eating (TRE), which imposes daily eating windows rather than full fasting days, yields more moderate but still significant reductions. TRE reduces body weight by 1\u20134% after 1\u201316 weeks in individuals with obesity relative to controls with no meal timing restrictions [16]. In a separate trial, a TRE group achieved approximately 3% to 4% reductions in weight, BMI, and trunk fat after three months [11], converging with the upper bound of the 1\u20134% range reported across TRE studies [16]. Over longer durations, a fasting group lost an average of 3.6% of body weight compared to the control group after six months [10], and after 12 months of time-restricted eating combined with resistance training, body mass decreased by 3.36% in the TRE group (P = 0.001, d = \u22120.27), while the normal diet group gained 3.37% [15]. The divergence between the TRE and normal diet trajectories at 12 months underscores that the clinical benefit of IF extends beyond acute caloric deficit to include prevention of age-related or dietary-pattern weight gain [15].\n\nThe only association supported by high-quality GRADE evidence in the BMI domain is that modified alternate-day fasting (MADF) for 1 to 2 months reduces BMI in healthy adults and adults with overweight, obesity, or non-alcoholic fatty liver disease compared with regular diet (MD \u22121.20; 95% CI \u22121.44 to \u22120.96) [18]. The tight confidence interval (spanning only 0.48 points) lends precision to this estimate and strengthens the clinical interpretability of the finding. At the shorter end of the temporal spectrum, Ramadan fasting produced a significant reduction in body mass of 1.87 kg (p = 0.001) after just four weeks [19], though the transient and culturally embedded nature of Ramadan protocols limits direct comparison with elective IF regimens. A broader meta-analytic synthesis concluded that all IF forms significantly reduced body weight compared to usual diets when considering direct evidence only [17], supporting the generalizability of weight loss across IF subtypes.\n\nHead-to-head comparisons between IF protocols are less common but increasingly available. In a randomized controlled trial of 101 overweight or obese adults with prediabetes assigned to ADF (n = 34), 16/8 time-restricted feeding (n = 33), or control (n = 34), both fasting groups showed significantly greater reductions in body weight, BMI, and waist circumference than the control group (all p < .05) [13]. Although the between-protocol comparison was not the primary endpoint, the fact that both ADF and 16/8 TRF outperformed the control on all three anthropometric measures suggests that the caloric deficit\u2014rather than the specific temporal pattern\u2014may be the principal driver of weight-related outcomes. This interpretation aligns with the convergence of percentage weight loss across diverse IF formats: 3\u20134% for TRE at three months [11], 3.6% for general fasting at six months [10], and 3.36% for TRE with resistance training at twelve months [15].\n\n| Protocol | Duration | Weight/BMI Outcome | Effect Size | Source |\n|----------|----------|-------------------|-------------|--------|\n| ADF | Ongoing (rate reported) | Weight reduction | 0.75 kg/week | [1] |\n| 5:2 IF | Ongoing (rate reported) | Weight reduction | 0.25 kg/week | [1] |\n| TRE | 1\u201316 weeks | Body weight reduction | 1\u20134% | [16] |\n| TRE | 3 months | Weight, BMI, trunk fat reduction | ~3\u20134% | [11] |\n| General fasting | 6 months | Body weight loss | 3.6% vs control | [10] |\n| TRE + resistance training | 12 months | Body mass decrease | \u22123.36% (P=0.001, d=\u22120.27) | [15] |\n| MADF | 1\u20132 months | BMI reduction | MD \u22121.20 (95% CI \u22121.44 to \u22120.96) | [18] |\n| Ramadan fasting | 4 weeks | Body mass reduction | \u22121.87 kg (p=0.001) | [19] |\n\nA critical concern in any weight-loss intervention is the preservation of lean body mass, as reductions in lean tissue can attenuate metabolic rate and compromise functional capacity. The available evidence on this point is reassuring. A systematic review of intermittent fasting combined with resistance training found that lean body mass was generally maintained, with one study reporting a significant increase in LBM [9]. Complementing this, investigators found no evidence that extending the daily fasting period negatively affected lean mass, consistent with most TRE studies [12]. The 12-month trial of TRE with resistance training further corroborates lean mass preservation: the significant decrease in total body mass (\u22123.36%) occurred without reported lean mass loss [15], suggesting that the weight reduction reflected primarily fat mass. These findings collectively indicate that IF, particularly when paired with resistance training, can achieve clinically meaningful fat loss while sparing metabolically active lean tissue [9][12][15].\n\nThe mechanistic basis for lean mass preservation during IF likely involves the interplay of fasting-induced hormonal shifts\u2014particularly sustained growth hormone secretion during fasting windows\u2014and the anabolic stimulus of resistance exercise. While growth hormone promotes lipolysis and protein sparing during caloric deficit, resistance training provides the mechanical loading signal necessary to maintain muscle protein synthesis [9][12]. This dual mechanism may explain why IF protocols without concurrent exercise training have shown more variable lean mass outcomes in some studies, though the present evidence base does not include direct comparisons of IF with versus without exercise on this endpoint.\n\nFrom a clinical and public health standpoint, the weight loss achieved through IF\u2014typically in the range of 3\u20134% over 3\u201312 months [10][11][15]\u2014falls within the threshold associated with meaningful cardiometabolic improvement. A meta-analysis of 25 clinical trial outcomes suggests that each 1-kg loss in body weight reduced systolic blood pressure by 1.05 mm Hg [14], implying that the 1.87 kg loss observed with four weeks of Ramadan fasting [19] could translate to an approximate 2 mm Hg reduction in SBP, and the larger losses achievable over 6\u201312 months could yield proportionally greater hemodynamic benefits. However, it must be noted that BMI and body weight are surrogate endpoints; patient-centered outcomes such as cardiovascular event reduction, diabetes incidence, or quality of life were not directly assessed in the trials reviewed here. The high-quality GRADE evidence supporting BMI reduction with MADF [18] provides a stronger evidentiary foundation than the moderate- or low-quality evidence underpinning some other IF protocols, which represents an important consideration for guideline development.\n\nSeveral limitations constrain the interpretability of the present findings. The Ramadan fasting data derive from a four-week observational window [19] that does not reflect the sustained adherence required for long-term weight management. The head-to-head RCT comparing ADF and 16/8 TRF enrolled adults with prediabetes [13], limiting generalizability to metabolically healthy populations. Few studies reported outcomes beyond 12 months, leaving the durability of weight loss uncertain. Additionally, the predominance of overweight and obese participants across trials [13][16][18] means that the magnitude and safety of weight loss in normal-weight individuals remain underexplored. The absence of patient-centered endpoints across the evidence base further limits the capacity to assess the real-world clinical significance of the observed anthropometric changes.\n\nFuture research should prioritize long-term randomized trials comparing IF protocols head-to-head over periods exceeding 12 months, with dual-energy X-ray absorptiometry or comparable gold-standard body composition assessment to definitively characterize lean mass preservation. Direct measurement of patient-centered outcomes\u2014cardiovascular events, diabetes remission, functional status\u2014rather than reliance on surrogate anthropometric markers would substantially advance the field. Investigations in diverse populations, including normal-weight and older adults at risk for sarcopenia, are needed to define the boundaries of IF's applicability and safety.\n\n**Key Findings**\n- Alternate-day fasting produces more rapid weight reduction (0.75 kg/week) than 5:2 intermittent fasting (0.25 kg/week), suggesting a dose-response relationship between fasting frequency and weight loss magnitude [1].\n- TRE consistently reduces body weight by 1\u20134% over 1\u201316 weeks in individuals with obesity [16], with reductions of approximately 3\u20134% in weight, BMI, and trunk fat at three months [11] and 3.36\u20133.6% at 6\u201312 months compared to controls [10][15].\n- The only IF\u2013BMI association supported by high-quality GRADE evidence is MADF for 1\u20132 months (MD \u22121.20; 95% CI \u22121.44 to \u22120.96) [18], while evidence for other protocols rests on moderate- or low-quality data.\n- Lean body mass is generally preserved during intermittent fasting, particularly when combined with resistance training [9][12][15].\n- Each 1-kg of weight loss through IF may reduce systolic blood pressure by approximately 1.05 mm Hg [14], linking the anthropometric benefits to downstream cardiometabolic improvement.\n\n---\n\n[1] - \u2713 used\n[9] - \u2713 used\n[10] - \u2713 used\n[11] - \u2713 used\n[12] - \u2713 used\n[13] - \u2713 used\n[14] - \u2713 used\n[15] - \u2713 used\n[16] - \u2713 used\n[17] - \u2713 used\n[18] - \u2713 used\n[19] - \u2713 used\n\nThat's 12 out of 12 sources!
