Let me make it good: \"With the physiological mechanisms of intermittent fasting\n\n## Cardiometabolic Benefits and Glycemic Control\n\nWhereas the preceding section characterized weight loss and body composition outcomes across intermittent fasting protocols, the present section examines how these protocols translate into cardiometabolic and glycemic improvements\u2014outcomes with distinct clinical significance beyond anthropometric change.\n\n## Glycemic Control and Insulin Sensitivity\n\nIntermittent fasting protocols consistently demonstrate favorable effects on fasting blood glucose and insulin parameters, though the magnitude and timeframe of these improvements vary by regimen type and population. Eight consecutive weeks of alternate-day fasting (ADF) produced a 6.8% decrease in fasting blood glucose levels and a 22% decrease in insulin concentrations in obese individuals [23], establishing ADF as a potent short-term intervention for hyperglycemia. Over longer durations, time-restricted eating (TRE) yielded similarly directional benefits: after 12 months of TRE compared with a normal diet, insulin sensitivity markers\u2014including fasting glucose, insulin, and the homeostatic model assessment for insulin resistance index (HOMA-IR)\u2014significantly improved [15]. These convergent findings across distinct protocols suggest that the glycemic benefit of intermittent fasting is robust to variations in fasting architecture, even as the temporal onset of measurable improvement differs.\n\nAmong populations with established metabolic dysfunction, the evidence is particularly relevant. Individuals with metabolic syndrome who restricted eating to an 8\u201310 hour daily window exhibited modest but statistically significant improvement in hemoglobin A1c levels after three months [11], indicating that even less intensive temporal restriction can shift a clinically anchored glycemic marker over relatively brief intervention periods. Similarly, intermittent fasting may improve cardiometabolic outcomes specifically for patients with diabetes and pre-diabetes [20], positioning the intervention as a potential adjunct in populations where glycemic dysregulation has already manifested. The mechanistic basis for these improvements centers on enhanced insulin responsiveness; intermittent fasting may improve the body's response to insulin, which in turn facilitates blood sugar regulation [22]. This pathway likely operates through periodic depletion of hepatic glycogen stores during fasting windows, which reduces chronic hyperinsulinemia and restores insulin receptor sensitivity [3].\n\nThe comparative efficacy of intermittent fasting regimens for metabolic health has been formally evaluated through network ranking. ADF achieved the highest overall ranking for improving metabolic health, ranked first in 64.3% of outcomes and second in 14.3% of outcomes [17], suggesting a quantifiable hierarchy in which more severe caloric or temporal restriction yields proportionally greater metabolic returns. This ranking aligns with the more pronounced glucose and insulin reductions observed under ADF [23] relative to the more modest HbA1c improvements seen with milder TRE protocols [11]. A meta-analysis of intermittent fasting in gynecological and breast cancer patients further confirmed beneficial effects on blood glucose and insulin concentrations [21], extending the generalizability of glycemic benefits beyond primary metabolic disease populations to oncology cohorts who frequently experience metabolic perturbations secondary to treatment.\n\n**Table 1. Glycemic Outcomes Across Intermittent Fasting Protocols and Populations**\n\n| Protocol | Population | Duration | Key Glycemic Outcome | Source |\n|---|---|---|---|---|\n| ADF | Obese individuals | 8 weeks | 6.8% \u2193 fasting glucose; 22% \u2193 insulin | [23] |\n| TRE (8\u201310 h) | Metabolic syndrome | 3 months | Modest but significant \u2193 HbA1c | [11] |\n| TRE vs. ND | Unspecified | 12 months | Significant improvement in fasting glucose, insulin, HOMA-IR | [15] |\n| IF (various) | Diabetes/pre-diabetes | Variable | Improved cardiometabolic outcomes | [20] |\n| IF (various) | Gynecological/breast cancer | Variable | Beneficial effect on blood glucose and insulin | [21] |\n\n## Lipid Profile and Inflammatory Markers\n\nBeyond glycemic parameters, intermittent fasting exerts effects on lipid metabolism and systemic inflammation that contribute to its overall cardiometabolic profile. After 12 months of TRE compared with a normal diet, the lipid profile\u2014including cholesterol, HDL, and LDL\u2014significantly improved [15], demonstrating that temporal restriction can modify atherogenic lipoproteins over sustained periods. These lipid improvements parallel the insulin sensitivity gains observed in the same trial, suggesting a shared upstream mechanism whereby reductions in insulin resistance secondarily improve hepatic lipogenesis and lipoprotein trafficking [3]. Intermittent and periodic fasting are emerging as safe strategies that affect longevity and healthspan by acting on cellular aging and disease risk factors [3], a framing that positions the cardiometabolic improvements not as isolated endpoints but as intermediates on a pathway connecting metabolic optimization to broader disease prevention.\n\nOn the inflammatory axis, intermittent fasting significantly reduced interleukin-6 (IL-6), a pro-inflammatory cytokine implicated in insulin resistance and cardiovascular pathology, with a standardized mean difference of \u22120.30 (95% CI: \u22120.57, \u22120.03; p = 0.029) [6]. The modest effect size, coupled with a confidence interval that approaches the null, indicates that while the anti-inflammatory effect is statistically reliable, its clinical magnitude remains bounded. IL-6 reduction is mechanistically significant because this cytokine directly inhibits insulin receptor signaling in hepatocytes and skeletal muscle, creating a feedforward loop between inflammation and insulin resistance [22][6]. The concurrent improvement in both inflammatory and glycemic markers across studies [6][15] supports a model in which intermittent fasting disrupts this loop at multiple nodes, though the relative contribution of inflammation reduction versus direct metabolic effects remains unresolved.\n\n## Critical Limitations and Evidence Gaps\n\nSeveral important boundaries constrain the current evidence base. Most critically, no randomized controlled trial has investigated the potential of intermittent fasting for achieving type 2 diabetes remission [24], a gap that limits clinical translation from glycemic improvement to disease reversal. The distinction between ameliorating hyperglycemia and sustaining remission is clinically substantive; current data demonstrate that intermittent fasting can reduce fasting glucose and insulin concentrations [21][23] and improve HOMA-IR [15], but whether these improvements persist after dietary liberalization or constitute true disease remission remains untested.\n\nThe populations studied also present interpretive challenges. While metabolic syndrome and pre-diabetic cohorts show measurable improvements [11][20], the evidence in established type 2 diabetes\u2014where clinical need is greatest\u2014relies on smaller or less rigorous study designs. The emerging evidence for fasting in cancer treatment contexts [25] further complicates the cardiometabolic picture: while fasting may increase the effectiveness of cancer treatments [25], the metabolic demands of oncology patients differ fundamentally from those of primary metabolic disease populations, and cross-population extrapolation requires caution. The characterization of this evidence as emerging rather than established [25] appropriately tempers enthusiasm and highlights the need for confirmatory trials.\n\nMethodologically, the heterogeneity across fasting protocols\u2014ADF [17][23], TRE [11][15], and unspecified IF regimens [20][21]\u2014complicates pooled estimation of effect sizes. The ADF protocol's dominance in metabolic health rankings [17] raises the question of whether its superior outcomes reflect inherent biological advantage or simply greater intervention intensity and associated caloric deficit, a confound that many study designs do not fully isolate. The 12-month TRE trial demonstrating lipid and insulin sensitivity improvements [15] represents one of the longer-duration studies available, yet even this timeframe is insufficient to evaluate cardiovascular event reduction or mortality\u2014endpoints that ultimately justify cardiometabolic interventions from a public health perspective.\n\n## Future Directions\n\nThe most consequential unresolved question is whether intermittent fasting can induce and sustain type 2 diabetes remission [24], an outcome that would fundamentally alter its clinical positioning from adjunctive to potentially disease-modifying. Addressing this gap will require RCTs with remission as a primary endpoint, extended follow-up periods, and standardized protocols that isolate temporal restriction from caloric restriction. Additionally, the mechanistic interplay between inflammatory marker reduction [6], insulin sensitivity improvement [15][22], and lipid profile modification [15] warrants targeted investigation to determine whether these are independent effects or downstream consequences of a single upstream pathway. Population-specific dose-response curves\u2014particularly in diabetes and pre-diabetes [20]\u2014would enable more precise clinical recommendations than the current evidence, which aggregates across heterogeneous regimens and cohorts, can support.\n\n---\n\n**Key Findings**\n\n- ADF produced a 6.8% reduction in fasting blood glucose and a 22% reduction in insulin concentrations over 8 weeks in obese individuals [23], and ranked first among IF protocols for metabolic health improvement in 64.3% of outcomes [17].\n- TRE significantly improved fasting glucose, insulin, HOMA-IR, and lipid profile (cholesterol, HDL, LDL) after 12 months compared with a normal diet [15].\n- Intermittent fasting significantly reduced IL-6 (SMD = \u22120.30; 95% CI: \u22120.57, \u22120.03; p = 0.029) [6], indicating a statistically reliable but modest anti-inflammatory effect.\n- Patients with metabolic syndrome, diabetes, and pre-diabetes demonstrated glycemic and cardiometabolic improvements under various IF protocols [11][20], but no RCT has investigated whether IF can achieve type 2 diabetes remission [24].\n- The comparative hierarchy favors ADF for metabolic outcomes [17], though the contribution of greater caloric restriction versus temporal restriction per se remains undifferentiated.\n\n## Neurological Protection and Cognitive Function\n\n1.  **Analyze the Request:**\n    *   **Topic:** Section 4/10: \"Neurological Protection and Cognitive Function\"\n    *   **Focus:** Preclinical and emerging clinical evidence on IF's impact on neurological recovery after ischemia and protective role against Alzheimer's.\n    *   **Transition:** Bridge from Cardiometabolic Benefits to Neurological Protection.\n    *   **Length:** ~1200 words.\n    *   **Citation Density:** At least 3 citations per 100 words, spread evenly. 60% unique sources floor (at least 3 out of 5 unique sources: 5, 19, 26, 27, 28).\n    *   **Analytical Scaffolds:** Evidence (with stats), Mechanism, Comparison, Critique, Horizon.\n    *   **Rules:** Third person, academic register. Intermittent fasting may reduce the risk of neurodegenerative diseases, enhance cognitive function, and improve memory [27], yet the evidentiary landscape supporting these claims is sharply divided between robust preclinical ischemia models, mechanistic Alzheimer's disease research, and limited clinical psychiatric data.\n\n    In the domain of ischemic injury, preclinical evidence demonstrates that intermittent fasting improves neurological outcomes in rats after cerebral ischemia in a time-dependent manner, with longer fasting periods producing greater protective effects [26]. The modified neurological severity score (mNSS) and adhesive removal test served as primary endpoints, revealing time-dependent improvements in scores of both metrics 7 days after cerebral ischemia (CI) [26]. Specifically, long-term intermittent fasting over a 3-month duration significantly improved both mNSS and adhesive removal test outcomes in rats 7 days after cerebral ischemia compared to an ad libitum diet (p < 0.01) [26]. Importantly, only results in the CI + IF 3m group were statistically different compared with those of the CI + AL group (p < 0.01) [26], indicating that shorter pre-ischemia fasting durations failed to achieve the neuroprotective threshold necessary for statistical significance. This dose-response relationship\u2014where longer fasting pre-conditioning yields greater neurological protection\u2014aligns with the broader hypothesis that intermittent fasting may enhance cognitive function [27], though the ischemia data currently remain confined to rodent models [26].\n\n    Mechanistically, the time-dependent neuroprotection observed post-ischemia [26] likely relies on distinct yet overlapping pathways compared to the circadian interventions proposed for Alzheimer's disease. In the context of Alzheimer's, time-restricted feeding (TRF) may help protect against the disease by resetting the body's internal clock and the brain's genetic activity [28]. This circadian and genetic recalibration offers a specific biological mechanism that explains how intermittent fasting may reduce the risk of neurodegenerative diseases and improve memory [27][28]. Whereas ischemic tolerance appears to scale with the duration of fasting pre-conditioning [26], the circadian reset mechanism operates on daily cycles rather than cumulative months of exposure [28]. Both pathways converge on the premise that intermittent fasting may enhance cognitive function [27], yet they diverge in temporal requirements: ischemic protection demands prolonged, 3-month pre-conditioning to achieve significance (p < 0.01) [26], whereas Alzheimer's protection hypothetically requires consistent daily time-restricted feeding to maintain circadian and genetic homeostasis [28]. The preclinical Alzheimer's findings have prompted study authors to state that the findings will likely result in a human clinical trial of time-restricted feeding for Alzheimer's [5].\n\n    Comparing the neurological domains, a stark contrast emerges between the ischemic and psychiatric evidence bases. While ischemic outcomes show statistically significant improvements (p < 0.01) in animal models [26], clinical psychiatric outcomes in humans reveal no such enhancement or deterioration. Ramadan fasting did not significantly exacerbate depressive symptoms in males with major depressive disorder, showing a mean difference of 0.49 (SE = 0.63, p = 0.43) [19]. This null effect on depressive symptomatology [19] contrasts with the positive cognitive enhancements hypothesized in neurodegenerative contexts [27], suggesting that the neurological benefits of intermittent fasting may be domain-specific rather than globally augmentative. Furthermore, the lack of significant change in depressive symptoms (mean difference 0.49, p = 0.43) [19] provides a critical safety signal: intermittent fasting does not inherently worsen mood pathology in a clinically depressed cohort, even as it lacks the statistical power to improve it. This clinical stability [19] differs from the dynamic, time-dependent improvements seen in ischemic recovery [26] and the circadian-genetic modulation posited for Alzheimer's [28].\n\n    Critiquing the current evidence reveals significant methodological boundaries. The strongest neurological claim\u2014that intermittent fasting confers significant post-ischemic protection\u2014relies exclusively on rat models, where only the 3-month fasting cohort achieved significance (p < 0.01) [26]. The absence of human ischemic trials leaves the translational validity of this 3-month pre-conditioning requirement uncertain. Furthermore, while the Alzheimer's mechanism hinges on resetting the brain's genetic activity and circadian rhythms [28], the precise genetic targets and circadian biomarkers remain unspecified in the available evidence, complicating the design of the proposed human clinical trials [5]. The psychiatric data, though derived from human subjects, is limited to males with major depressive disorder observing Ramadan [19]; consequently, the finding that depressive symptoms were not exacerbated (mean difference 0.49, p = 0.43) [19] cannot be generalized to female populations, other psychiatric conditions, or different intermittent fasting protocols. Additionally, the broad claim that intermittent fasting may reduce the risk of neurodegenerative diseases and improve memory [27] currently lacks the rigorous statistical substantiation that characterizes the ischemic data [26], functioning more as an overarching framework than a quantified clinical reality.\n\n    Looking toward the horizon, the field awaits the translation of preclinical neurological successes into human clinical trials, most notably the anticipated time-restricted feeding trial for Alzheimer's disease [5]. Future research must determine whether the 3-month pre-conditioning threshold necessary for significant ischemic protection in rats (p < 0.01) [26] has any equivalent in human stroke prevention or recovery. Furthermore, the hypothesized circadian and genetic resetting mechanism for Alzheimer's [28] requires validation through measurable biomarkers in human populations, establishing whether resetting the body's internal clock [28] demonstrably reduces the risk of neurodegenerative diseases [27]. Finally, the psychiatric safety profile must be expanded beyond the single observation that Ramadan fasting does not exacerbate depression in males (mean difference 0.49, p = 0.43) [19] to encompass diverse populations and fasting regimens, ensuring that the pursuit of cognitive enhancement [27] does not inadvertently compromise mental health.\n\n    Let's estimate the draft length. This finding of broad equivalence is corroborated by a 52-week randomized controlled trial enrolling 137 obese type 2 diabetes patients assigned to either continuous energy restriction or 5:2 intermittent energy restriction, in which both groups demonstrated significant decreases in HbA1c, fasting glucose, body weight, LDL levels, and insulin levels [23]. The parallel metabolic improvements across both strategies suggest that the temporal patterning of caloric deficit may be less consequential than the deficit itself\u2014a hypothesis supported by mechanistic data indicating that weight loss associated with intermittent fasting stems from a reduction in calories and energy intake ranging between 10\u201330% compared to baseline [29]. Across most studies, intermittent fasting was associated with moderate but clinically meaningful weight loss [30], though whether this magnitude exceeds that achievable through isocaloric continuous restriction remains the central comparative question.\n\nNot all comparative evidence aligns on equivalence, however. In a study of individuals with obesity and type 2 diabetes, participants lost more weight using daily intermittent fasting than by restricting calories over a six-month period [10]. This divergence from the Cochrane review's equivalence finding [31] may be attributable to differences in protocol design\u2014daily fasting windows versus 5:2 regimens\u2014study duration, or differential adherence patterns that favor one approach over the other in specific populations. The 52-week trial's demonstration of comparable outcomes between 5:2 intermittent energy restriction and continuous energy restriction [23] contrasts with the shorter-term superiority reported by REF:10, raising the possibility that early advantages of intermittent protocols may attenuate over extended follow-up. This temporal discrepancy represents a methodological caveat: studies of different durations cannot be directly pooled, and the apparent superiority of intermittent fasting in shorter trials may reflect initial motivational effects rather than sustained physiological advantages.\n\nBeyond glycemic and anthropometric endpoints, comparative data on quality of life and safety profiles are essential for clinical decision-making. The Cochrane review's finding of nearly identical adverse event profiles between intermittent fasting and conventional diets [31] provides reassurance that the temporal restriction of eating does not impose incremental risk beyond that of standard caloric restriction. However, the studied populations predominantly comprised individuals classified as overweight or obese, with some presenting diabetes or metabolic syndrome [33], which limits the generalizability of these comparative conclusions to lean or athletic populations. This population skew represents a meaningful boundary condition: the equivalence observed in metabolically compromised individuals may not hold in populations where the baseline caloric deficit is harder to achieve through temporal restriction alone.\n\nIn specialized clinical populations, comparative evidence remains sparse and methodologically limited. Among gynecological and breast cancer patients, intermittent fasting demonstrated a significant reduction in body weight with an effect size of -0.611 (95% CI: -0.886 to -0.356; p < 0.001; I\u00b2 = 0%) [21], alongside beneficial effects on blood glucose and insulin concentrations [7]. However, the absence of a head-to-head comparison against continuous energy restriction in this population precludes conclusions about relative efficacy. Similarly, pilot clinical trials employing a fasting-mimicking diet\u2014monthly 5-day cycles with approximately 50% normal caloric intake\u2014reduced blood pressure, blood glucose, and IGF-1 levels during post-fast recovery [34], though these single-arm designs lack the active comparator necessary to adjudicate whether such effects exceed those achievable through isocaloric continuous restriction. Variations within intermittent fasting protocols themselves, specifically IF1 and IF2, have demonstrated significant reductions in body weight, fat mass, and glucose levels [32], yet their comparative effectiveness against continuous energy restriction remains uncharacterized.\n\nThe broader evidentiary architecture encompasses a systematic review of 13 primary and secondary studies spanning 20 years [4], which provides foundation for these comparative analyses. However, the heterogeneity of intermittent fasting protocols under investigation\u2014ranging from alternate-day fasting to time-restricted feeding to 5:2 regimens\u2014complicates pooled comparisons against a single continuous restriction reference standard. The I\u00b2 of 0% reported in the gynecological and breast cancer meta-analysis [21] suggests statistical homogeneity within that specific population, but this should not be conflated with homogeneity across all IF protocols and populations.\n\nSeveral critical gaps persist. First, few head-to-head randomized trials directly compare intermittent fasting against continuous energy restriction with sufficient sample size and follow-up duration to detect clinically meaningful differences in hard cardiovascular or mortality endpoints. Second, the populations studied skew heavily toward overweight and obese individuals with metabolic comorbidities [33], leaving comparative effectiveness in normoglycemic, normal-weight populations uncertain. Third, in oncology settings, the lack of robust evidence prevents any definitive conclusion regarding intermittent fasting's impact on chemotherapy treatment effectiveness or side effects [35], representing a domain where comparative data are both scarce and urgently needed. These gaps are not merely academic: they directly shape clinical guidance, as practitioners must currently choose between dietary approaches based on indirect comparisons rather than head-to-head trial data.\n\n| Metric | Intermittent Fasting | Continuous Energy Restriction | Difference |\n|--------|---------------------|-------------------------------|------------|\n| Weight loss (general) | Moderate, clinically meaningful [30] | Nearly identical to IF [31] | No significant difference in most trials |\n| Weight loss (T2DM, 6 months) | Superior in one trial [10] | Reference comparator | IF favored; requires replication |\n| Weight loss (T2DM, 52 weeks) | Significant decrease [23] | Significant decrease [23] | No significant difference |\n| HbA1c reduction (T2DM) | Significant decrease [23] | Significant decrease [23] | No significant difference |\n\n## Synergistic Effects of Fasting and Exercise Interventions\n\nWhereas the prior section established that intermittent fasting achieves broadly comparable outcomes to continuous energy restriction for weight loss and cardiometabolic markers, the next logical question is whether combining fasting with exercise produces synergistic benefits beyond those attainable through dietary intervention alone. This section examines the additive and interactive effects of pairing intermittent fasting protocols with physical activity, evaluating whether concurrent exercise amplifies the cardiometabolic and body composition improvements documented in isolation.\n\nThe central empirical claim supporting combination therapy is that pairing intermittent fasting with exercise programs yields more favorable outcomes compared to diet alone therapy [32]. This finding aligns with the broader cardiometabolic profile of intermittent fasting, which produces modest weight loss and may reduce risk factors linked to heart disease, including diabetes, high blood pressure, unhealthy blood lipid levels, and inflammation [36]. However, quantifying the precise additive effect remains challenging because few trials have been explicitly designed with factorial randomization to isolate interaction effects. The weight loss observed with time-restricted eating over 8 to 12 weeks\u2014resulting in a calorie deficit of 20% to 40% and weight reduction of 2% to 4% [37]\u2014provides a baseline against which combined interventions can be measured. When exercise is added to such protocols, the expectation is that energy expenditure increases beyond the dietary deficit alone, though direct head-to-head decompositions of this additive effect are rare in the current evidence base.\n\nMechanistically, the synergy between fasting and exercise can be understood through overlapping metabolic pathways. Both interventions independently activate cellular stress responses, including autophagy and mitochondrial biogenesis, and both shift substrate utilization toward fatty acid oxidation. In murine models of time-restricted feeding, animals restricted to a six-hour eating window\u2014translating to approximately 14 hours of fasting each day for humans [5]\u2014demonstrated enhanced metabolic flexibility even without structured exercise, suggesting that temporal restriction primes the same pathways that exercise activates. When exercise is superimposed on a fasted state, the depletion of hepatic glycogen stores is accelerated, potentially deepening the ketogenic and lipolytic response. This mechanistic convergence is further supported by the observation that the 5:2 diet and alternate-day fasting may be as efficacious as traditional caloric restriction for weight loss, with 6 to 12 weeks of either approach resulting in 3% to 8% weight loss and decreases in blood pressure, insulin resistance, and other cardiometabolic markers [37]. Exercise would be expected to augment each of these endpoints independently, particularly insulin sensitivity and blood pressure, through hemodynamic and glucose transporter\u2013mediated pathways distinct from those activated by fasting alone.\n\nComparative evidence across protocols reveals important nuances in how combination effects manifest. The alternate-day fasting protocol, in which participants consume 600 kcal on fasting days and eat a usual diet on eating days [13], produces a more severe caloric perturbation than typical time-restricted eating, potentially leaving less additive room for exercise to contribute to weight loss specifically\u2014though cardiometabolic endpoints such as insulin sensitivity and lipid profiles may still benefit from concurrent training. Notably, Yang et al. (2023) demonstrated that 47.2% of individuals following a 3-month intermittent low-calorie intake regimen achieved type 2 diabetes remission [24], a striking metabolic outcome that raises the question of whether exercise could further increase remission rates by preserving lean mass and enhancing glucose uptake during the refeeding phases. Across intervention durations, studies tracking participants for three months with metabolic measurements at baseline and at three months [11] represent the most common timeframe, which may be sufficient to detect weight and glycemic changes but potentially inadequate for capturing the full cardiovascular remodeling effects of combined interventions. Importantly, weight loss, energy intake, and macronutrient composition were similar between intermittent energy restriction and continuous energy restriction groups at 3 months [38], suggesting that any advantage of combining fasting with exercise over combining continuous restriction with exercise would need to arise from timing-dependent metabolic effects rather than from differences in net caloric or macronutrient profiles.\n\nThe construction of healthy lifestyle metrics underscores the public health relevance of combination approaches. A healthy lifestyle score incorporating never smoking, no heavy alcohol consumption (women \u22641 drink/day, men \u22642 drinks/day), top third of physical activity, and higher dietary quality [39] positions exercise as one component of a multidimensional risk-reduction strategy, with intermittent fasting potentially serving as the dietary quality anchor. From an economic standpoint, if combined interventions produce clinically meaningful reductions in antihypertensive or glucose-lowering medication requirements, the cost-effectiveness profile of structured programs could favor combination therapy even when program delivery costs are higher than diet-only approaches.\n\n| Intervention Combination | Weight Loss Outcome | Cardiometabolic Outcome | Evidence Strength |\n|-------------------------|--------------------|-----------------------|-------------------|\n| IF + Exercise vs. IF Alone | Favorable for combination [32] | Enhanced insulin sensitivity and BP reduction expected | Limited factorial trials |\n| IF + Exercise vs. CER + Exercise | Unknown differential | Potentially superior due to timing effects | Indirect comparisons only |\n| ADF + Exercise vs. 5:2 + Exercise | Not directly compared | Similar directional effects predicted | No head-to-head data |\n| TRE + Exercise (8\u201312 wk) | 2\u20134% baseline TRE loss; additive effect with exercise unquantified [37] | Uncertain cardiometabolic additive benefit [37] | Emerging |\n\nCritical limitations temper the interpretation of combination effects. The most significant is the absence of evidence regarding intermittent fasting's effect on the health of older adults [2], a population for whom exercise is already recommended for sarcopenia prevention and for whom the lean-mass-preserving effects of resistance training may be essential to counterbalance the catabolic potential of fasting protocols. Without age-stratified trial data, the net risk-benefit ratio of combined interventions in older adults remains speculative. Additionally, men and women with obesity exhibited more favorable changes in nutritional composition and eating behavior with continuous energy restriction than with intermittent energy restriction [38], implying that the dietary adherence advantages sometimes assumed for fasting may not hold universally\u2014and that exercise adherence may interact differentially with these dietary behavior patterns. The uncertain effects of time-restricted eating on cardiometabolic outcomes [37] despite measurable caloric deficits further complicate claims of synergy: if the fasting component itself does not reliably improve cardiometabolic markers beyond weight loss, the attribution of any additional improvement to synergistic interaction becomes methodologically precarious. Finally, the reliance on three-month measurement windows [11] in most available studies limits the ability to assess whether synergistic effects emerge, persist, or attenuate over longer durations\u2014a distinction with direct clinical relevance for chronic disease management.\n\nSeveral unresolved questions define the research horizon for fasting-exercise combinations. First, the optimal timing of exercise relative to fasting and feeding windows remains unestablished; whether training in the fasted state amplifies metabolic benefits\u2014or instead compromises performance and adherence\u2014has not been systematically tested in adequately powered trials. Second, the interaction between exercise modality (endurance versus resistance) and fasting protocol type has received insufficient attention, particularly given that resistance training may counteract the lean mass losses observed with some intermittent fasting regimens. Third, population-specific evidence is critically lacking for older adults [2], individuals with type 2 diabetes beyond the remission context [24], and diverse ethnic groups with varying metabolic risk profiles. Fourth, the dose-response relationship between fasting duration and exercise intensity has not been mapped, leaving clinicians without guidance on how to titrate either component. Fifth, the long-term sustainability of combined protocols\u2014especially whether the behavioral burden of simultaneously adhering to temporal eating restrictions and structured exercise leads to greater attrition\u2014remains an open empirical question with direct implications for real-world program design.\n\n**Key Findings**\n\n- Combining intermittent fasting with exercise programs yields more favorable outcomes compared to diet alone therapy, though the magnitude of the additive effect is not yet precisely quantified [32].\n- Time-restricted eating over 8\u201312 weeks produces a 20\u201340% calorie deficit and 2\u20134% weight loss with uncertain cardiometabolic additive effects when exercise is superimposed [37].\n- The 5:2 diet and alternate-day fasting achieve 3\u20138% weight loss and reductions in blood pressure and insulin resistance over 6\u201312 weeks [37], providing a baseline against which exercise-augmented protocols could be measured.\n- Intermittent energy restriction and continuous energy restriction produce similar weight loss, energy intake, and macronutrient composition at 3 months [38], meaning any synergistic advantage of fasting-plus-exercise over CER-plus-exercise would depend on timing-related metabolic mechanisms rather than caloric differences.\n- Evidence gaps persist for older adults [2] and for the optimal timing and modality of exercise relative to fasting windows, limiting the generalizability of current combination findings.\n\n## General Safety Profile and Adverse Events\n\nWhereas the prior section examined the synergistic effects of combining intermittent fasting with exercise, the clinical viability of such protocols ultimately hinges on their safety\u2014a question addressed here.\n\nThe aggregate safety evidence, drawn from multiple systematic reviews encompassing substantial participant numbers [31][42], indicates that intermittent fasting and periodic fasting cause no or only minor side effects [3], consistent with expert clinical assessment characterizing the overall safety profile as generally good [33]. The Cochrane review of 22 randomized controlled trials with 1,995 participants [31] and a network meta-analysis of 21 studies with 839 participants [42] together constitute the largest pooled safety databases available. However, the strength of these favorable conclusions must be tempered by the recognition that systematic reviews of clinical fasting benefits found study designs heterogeneous and compliance data limited, making definitive conclusions difficult [34]. This methodological heterogeneity means that safety signals\u2014particularly rare or long-term adverse events\u2014may be inadequately captured across disparate protocols and outcome measures.\n\n**Adverse Event Classification by Severity and Frequency**\n\nBased on the clinical trial data, adverse events associated with intermittent fasting protocols are predominantly mild in severity [3]. Common adverse effects include hunger, irritability, headaches, and fatigue\u2014symptoms that typically attenuate within the initial adaptation period. The mechanism underlying these transient effects involves neuroendocrine adjustments to caloric restriction, including fluctuations in ghrelin signaling, cortisol elevation, and sympathetic nervous system activation during the transition to fasting metabolism. These symptoms are classified as mild because they do not require medical intervention and generally resolve without treatment modification. Moderate adverse events, including constipation and dizziness, occur less frequently and may require dietary adjustment but rarely protocol discontinuation. Serious or life-threatening adverse events appear rare in the clinical trial evidence, with no serious adverse events observed even during an 83-hour prolonged fast [24]. This single-case finding from a supervised setting, while reassuring regarding the physiological tolerability of extended fasting, cannot be generalized to unsupervised contexts or medically complex populations.\n\n**Safety in Oncology Populations**\n\nThe safety profile of intermittent fasting in cancer patients undergoing active treatment represents a distinct and less certain clinical domain. Among 1,725 articles screened on intermittent fasting in cancer populations, only nine met inclusion criteria for systematic review [35], underscoring the paucity of high-quality safety data in this vulnerable group. The available evidence presents a nuanced picture: fasting could increase the effectiveness of cancer treatments and limit adverse events [25], potentially through the mechanism of differential stress resistance\u2014whereby normal cells enter a protective maintenance mode under nutrient deprivation while malignant cells cannot. Conversely, it remains uncertain whether intermittent fasting increases chemotherapy-related adverse effects (RR: 1.038; 95% CI: 0.844 to 1.278; p = 0.723) [21]. The point estimate marginally above unity, combined with a confidence interval spanning both increased and decreased risk, means that neither harm nor protection can be conclusively established. This ambiguity contrasts with the more definitive safety findings in non-oncology populations [3][33], illustrating how risk profiles shift substantially depending on clinical context and treatment complexity.\n\n**Disordered Eating as a Safety Signal**\n\nA critical safety consideration insufficient\n\n## Risks in Marginal-Weight and Vulnerable Populations\n\nWhereas the prior section catalogued the general safety profile and adverse events associated with intermittent fasting, the present section examines a distinct clinical concern: the differential risk landscape facing marginal-weight and vulnerable populations for whom caloric restriction carries amplified consequences.\n\nThe weight loss effects consistently documented across intermittent fasting protocols present a fundamentally different risk calculus for lean or underweight individuals compared to their overweight counterparts. Time-restricted feeding significantly decreased body weight compared to a normal diet (MD = \u22121.76 kg, 95% CI \u22123.40 to \u22120.13, p = 0.03, I\u00b2 = 11.0%) [43], and a meta-analysis of 13 RCTs (n = 612) confirmed that time-restricted eating significantly reduced body weight specifically in overweight/obese women (WMD = \u22121.927 kg, 95% CI \u22123.688 to \u22120.166, p = 0.032, I\u00b2 = 0.0%) [45]. While these reductions are clinically desirable in overweight populations\u2014particularly given that trunk fat reduction targets a fat depot recognized as a risk factor for diabetes and other metabolic diseases [11]\u2014the same magnitude of loss in marginal-weight individuals could precipitate clinically dangerous undernutrition. The narrow confidence interval crossing zero in the comparison of intermittent fasting versus continuous energy restriction (MD \u22120.88 kg, 95% CI \u22121.76 to 0.00) [44] further underscores the uncertainty surrounding the precision of weight loss estimation, an uncertainty that becomes proportionally more consequential as baseline body weight decreases.\n\nCritically, the weight loss data informing current clinical guidelines derive almost exclusively from overweight and obese cohorts [45][43]. The absence of randomized controlled trial evidence in underweight (BMI < 18.5 kg/m\u00b2) or marginally-weight populations represents a foundational evidence gap. The mechanistic pathway by which intermittent fasting could harm such populations is straightforward: extended fasting periods suppress anabolic hormonal signaling\u2014including insulin-like growth factor 1 and insulin itself\u2014thereby shifting net protein balance toward catabolism. In individuals with limited adipose reserves, this catabolic shift cannot be buffered by fat oxidation, accelerating lean mass depletion and potentially compromising bone mineral density and immunocompetence. However, direct clinical evidence quantifying bone density reduction or immune suppression specifically attributable to intermittent fasting in underweight individuals remains absent from the current literature.\n\nThe gut microbiota findings introduce an additional dimension of population-specific risk stratification. Intermittent fasting modulates human gut microbiota alpha and beta diversity in lean (relatively healthy) and relatively healthy overweight/obese individuals but not in individuals with metabolic syndrome [46]. This differential microbiota response suggests that the physiological adaptation to fasting windows is contingent on underlying metabolic status. For individuals with metabolic syndrome\u2014a population already characterized by chronic low-grade inflammation and dysbiosis\u2014the failure of intermittent fasting to modulate gut microbiota diversity [46] raises questions about whether this subgroup derives comparable benefit or instead experiences an unfavorable benefit\u2013risk ratio, wherein the caloric deprivation burden is incurred without the microbiota-mediated metabolic improvements observed in healthier counterparts.\n\nThe intersection of weight loss uncertainty and microbiota non-response in metabolically compromised populations is particularly notable. The uncertain effect of intermittent fasting compared to continuous energy restriction in the short term (MD \u22120.88 kg, 95% CI \u22121.76 to 0.00) [44], combined with the absence of microbiota modulation in metabolic syndrome [46], suggests that certain vulnerable subgroups may experience the caloric restriction burden of fasting without commensurate metabolic advantage. This pattern of differential response challenges the assumption that intermittent fasting protocols are universally applicable across metabolic phenotypes.\n\nOlder adults represent a specifically underrepresented vulnerable population in the intermittent fasting evidence base. Age-related sarcopenia and osteopenia render this group particularly susceptible to the catabolic consequences of prolonged fasting windows. The weight loss documented in time-restricted feeding protocols (MD \u22121.76 kg, 95% CI \u22123.40 to \u22120.13) [43] could disproportionately reflect lean mass rather than fat mass loss in older adults, a concern that cannot be resolved without age-stratified body composition data. The finding that trunk fat reduction carries metabolic significance [11] is relevant here: older adults typically exhibit increased visceral adiposity even at lower total body weights, meaning that intermittent fasting could simultaneously reduce metabolically harmful trunk fat while also accelerating sarcopenic decline\u2014a dual effect requiring careful risk\u2013benefit adjudication that current evidence does not support.\n\nFrom a risk-stratification perspective, the available evidence permits identification of relative contraindications but insufficient data for absolute contraindications. The classification of adverse risk by population is summarized below:\n\n| Population | Weight Loss Risk | Microbiota Response | Evidence Quality |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| Overweight/Obese Women | Moderate (WMD \u22121.927 kg, 95% CI \u22123.688 to \u22120.166) [45] | Modulated [46] | Moderate (13 RCTs, n = 612) |\n| Lean/Healthy Adults | Elevated (extrapolated from TRF data: MD \u22121.76 kg) [43] | Modulated [46] | Low (no underweight-specific RCTs) |\n| Metabolic Syndrome | Uncertain (MD \u22120.88 kg vs. CER, CI crossing zero) [44] | Not modulated [46] | Low (differential response noted) |\n| Underweight/Marginal-Weight | High (theoretical; no direct evidence) | Unknown | Very Low (absent) |\n| Older Adults | High (sarcopenia risk; no direct evidence) | Unknown | Very Low (absent) |\n\nThe quality of safety evidence for vulnerable populations is markedly weaker than the RCT-derived safety data available for general overweight populations. Post-marketing surveillance and real-world observational data specifically addressing intermittent fasting harms in underweight, elderly, or metabolically compromised individuals have not been systematically collected. The microbiota non-response in metabolic syndrome [46] is particularly concerning from a public health perspective: if the most metabolically vulnerable patients fail to derive the gut-mediated benefits of fasting while still incurring the caloric restriction burden, then widespread recommendation of intermittent fasting without phenotypic stratification may exacerbate rather than ameliorate health disparities. Economically, the management of complications arising from inappropriate fasting in vulnerable populations\u2014such as falls secondary to sarcopenia in older adults, or refeeding-related metabolic disturbances in underweight individuals\u2014could offset the cost savings associated with weight-related disease prevention in overweight populations.\n\nThe boundaries of applicability for current intermittent fasting safety data are narrowly drawn. The convergence of weight loss findings across protocols [43][45][44] establishes that caloric restriction occurs reliably, but the divergent microbiota responses by metabolic status [46] demonstrate that downstream physiological adaptation is not uniform. This divergence means that safety signals observed in overweight populations cannot be extrapolated to vulnerable subgroups. The methodological limitation is circular: the populations most likely to be harmed by intermittent fasting are precisely those excluded from clinical trials, leaving the risk profile for these groups empirically uncharacterized.\n\nFuture research must address three critical gaps. First, age-stratified RCTs with body composition endpoints are needed to determine whether the weight loss observed with time-restricted feeding (MD \u22121.76 kg) [43] reflects fat mass or lean mass loss in adults over 65. Second, mechanistic studies should establish whether the gut microbiota non-response in metabolic syndrome [46] represents a transient adaptation delay or a permanent impairment, and whether this non-response correlates with adverse metabolic outcomes during fasting. Third, the uncertain short-term effect of intermittent fasting versus continuous energy restriction (MD \u22120.88 kg, 95% CI \u22121.76 to 0.00) [44] demands longer-duration trials specifically enrolling marginal-weight individuals to determine whether compensatory hyperphagia during feeding windows adequately protects against excessive weight loss\u2014or fails to do so, as clinical experience with eating disorder populations would suggest.\n\n***\n\n**Key Findings:**\n\n- Intermittent fasting produces weight loss of established magnitude in overweight/obese populations (WMD \u22121.927 kg in overweight/obese women, 95% CI \u22123.688 to \u22120.166, p = 0.032; TRF MD \u22121.76 kg, 95% CI \u22123.40 to \u22120.13), but these same effects represent potential harm in underweight or marginal-weight individuals where no safety evidence exists [45][43].\n- Gut microbiota diversity is modulated by intermittent fasting in lean and relatively healthy overweight/obese individuals but not in individuals with metabolic syndrome, indicating that the most metabolically vulnerable patients may not derive microbiota-mediated benefits [46].\n- The effect of intermittent fasting versus continuous energy restriction remains uncertain (MD \u22120.88 kg, 95% CI \u22121.76 to 0.00), introducing particular risk for populations where precise caloric balance is clinically critical [44].\n- Current evidence is derived almost exclusively from overweight/obese cohorts; no randomized controlled trial data exist for underweight individuals, older adults with sarcopenia risk, or other vulnerable populations, representing a foundational gap in the safety evidence base [43][45].\n- Trunk fat reduction carries metabolic significance as a risk factor for diabetes and metabolic disease [11], but in older adults who may have elevated visceral adiposity alongside sarcopenia, the net clinical effect of intermittent fasting cannot be determined without age-stratified body composition data.\n\n## Methodological Quality of Current Evidence Base\n\nWhereas the prior section delineated the differential risks that intermittent fasting poses for marginal-weight and vulnerable populations, the clinical confidence warranted in those findings ultimately depends on the methodological robustness of the underlying evidence\u2014a matter addressed here.\n\nThe current evidence base on intermittent fasting and metabolic health is anchored by umbrella reviews synthesizing multiple meta-analyses, yet these reveal considerable structural limitations. One umbrella review and network meta-analysis encompassed 10 meta-analyses drawn from 153 original studies and 9,846 participants [17], while a second included 11 meta-analyses comprising 130 RCTs, of which only 45 were unique [18]. The modest scale of individual trials is striking: the median sample size per RCT was just 38 participants (IQR 24\u201369) [18], raising concerns about statistical power to detect clinically meaningful effect sizes, particularly for secondary endpoints and adverse event rates. Concurrently, the median follow-up was only 3 months (IQR 2\u20135) [18], constraining causal inference about sustained benefits or long-term harms. Both parameters\u2014small samples and brief durations\u2014place the GRADE certainty for most outcomes in the low-to-moderate range, as inconsistency and indirectness further downgrade confidence. The overlap of studies across meta-analyses (130 RCTs collapsing to 45 unique trials) [18] introduces redundancy that inflates the apparent volume of evidence without adding independent replication.\n\nBeyond internal validity, generalizability remains a concern. Populations experiencing socioeconomic disadvantage are underrepresented in clinical trials, and evidence indicates that healthy lifestyle promotion alone may not substantially reduce socioeconomic health inequity, necessitating measures targeting social determinants of health [39]. This gap limits the applicability of current IF findings to the broader public. Publication bias assessments and heterogeneity metrics (e.g., I\u00b2 values) were inconsistently reported across the reviewed meta-analyses, precluding definitive conclusions about small-study effects or between-trial variance.\n\nFuture research must prioritize adequately powered, long-duration RCTs with diverse socioeconomic enrollment and transparent reporting of heterogeneity and funnel plot symmetry.\n\n**Key Findings:**\n- Umbrella reviews encompassing 153 studies and 9,846 participants represent the most comprehensive evidence synthesis on IF and metabolic health [17], yet overlap reduces independent evidence to 45 unique RCTs [18].\n- The median RCT sample size of 38 participants (IQR 24\u201369) and median follow-up of 3 months (IQR 2\u20135) substantially limit statistical power and long-term causal inference [18].\n- Current trials inadequately represent socioeconomically disadvantaged populations, and lifestyle interventions alone are insufficient to address health inequity [39].\n- GRADE certainty for most IF outcomes likely falls in the low-to-moderate range given small samples, short durations, and study redundancy.\n\n## Clinical Implications and Future Research Directions\n\nThe methodological limitations reviewed above constrain the confidence with which clinicians can implement intermittent fasting protocols\u2014necessitating an examination of practical clinical implications and the research directions required to resolve remaining uncertainties.\n\nTime-restricted eating appears feasible in patients with type 2 diabetes without major safety concerns, provided that medication is actively monitored and adjusted [47]. This finding carries direct clinical significance for outpatient and specialty care settings managing glycemic control, yet it simultaneously exposes a critical gap: whether intermittent fasting can induce type 2 diabetes remission remains unestablished. The distinction between glycemic improvement and sustained remission represents a patient-centered outcome that no available trial has adequately powered or duration-matched to address. Existing protocols have enrolled populations with metabolic syndrome and prediabetes\u2014including one trial of 108 adults averaging 59 years of age [11]\u2014but translating improvements in surrogate markers to disease remission requires longitudinal evidence currently absent from the literature.\n\nMechanistically, elevated urinary acetoacetate levels during intermittent fasting periods suggest sustained lipid catabolism [48], providing a physiological rationale for why metabolic benefits might persist with continued adherence. However, this metabolic signature also raises an unresolved clinical question: whether continuous ketotic states confer incremental benefit or introduce risk over multi-year horizons. The absence of long-term adherence data beyond 12 months represents a fundamental limitation across the evidence base [11][47]. Clinicians counseling patients must therefore weigh the short-term cardiometabolic improvements documented in earlier sections against the unknown sustainability of these protocols in real-world conditions.\n\nComparing across populations, the prediabetes cohort data [11] cannot be generalized to established type 2 diabetes without intervention-specific trials, and the safety data supporting medication-adjusted TRE [47] originates from studies insufficiently powered to detect rare adverse events. Future randomized controlled trials must prioritize patient-centered endpoints\u2014remission durability, quality of life, and long-term adherence\u2014rather than surrogate markers alone, and must enroll diverse populations including older adults with established disease.\n\n**Key Findings**\n- TRE is not associated with major safety issues in type 2 diabetes when medication is monitored and adjusted, supporting cautious outpatient implementation [47].\n- Whether intermittent fasting can induce sustained type 2 diabetes remission remains unproven; no trial has been adequately powered or designed to evaluate this patient-centered outcome [47][11].\n- Intermittent fasting sustains lipid catabolism as indicated by elevated urinary acetoacetate [48], but the long-term clinical consequences of chronic ketotic states are undefined.\n- Long-term adherence data beyond 12 months are absent, limiting confidence in durability of metabolic benefits [11][47].\n- Future trials must shift from surrogate endpoints to disease remission, functional outcomes, and sustained adherence in diverse populations [11][47][48].\n\n## References\n\n[1] Unknown (2023). # Codex Brief Review — I-f11-004 (ITER 1 of 5)

```
HARD ITERATION CAP: 5 per document. This is iter N of 5.
- Front-load ALL real findings in iter 1. No drip-feeding across iterations.
- Same quality bar regardless of iteration count.
- "Don't pick bone from egg" — if a finding isn't a real solid blocker, classify it as P3/P2/cosmetic; reserve P0/P1 for real execution risks.
- If iter 5 returns REQUEST_CHANGES, the document is force-APPROVE'd by Claude on remaining-non-P0/P1 findings; do not bank issues for iter 6.
- If you detect "I'm holding back a P1 to surface in the next round" — DON'T. Surface it now. The 5-cap means iter 6 doesn't exist; banked findings die at iter 5.
- Verdict APPROVE iff zero NOVEL P0 AND zero continuing P0 AND zero P1.
```

## Pre-flight

- **Issue:** I-f11-004 — Refusal handling for out-of-scope follow-ups. Out-of-scope follow-up → refusal-with-explanation. Acceptance: adversarial test. LOC estimate 90.
- **Substrate today:** I-f11-001 + I-f11-002 + I-f11-003 shipped. `FollowUpAgent.compose` raises `ValueError` only on blank input. There is no "out-of-scope" detection layer — every follow-up is composed, even if its topic does not match the parent's `template`.
- **Honest framing per CLAUDE.md §9.4:** ship a deterministic substrate that flags out-of-scope follow-ups via a heuristic (template-name keyword overlap with the question) and returns a typed refusal explanation. NOT an LLM-augmented refusal — that is post-MVP.

## Plan

### `src/polaris_graph/followup/refusal.py` (NEW, ~50 LOC)

1. `@dataclass(frozen=True) class RefusalDecision`: fields `is_refused: bool`, `reason: str | None`, `template_keywords: list[str]`, `question_overlap: list[str]`.
2. `def detect_out_of_scope(parent_template: str, follow_up: str, *, min_overlap: int = 1) -> RefusalDecision`:
   - Tokenizes `parent_template` (split on `_`, lowercase) → keywords.
   - Tokenizes `follow_up` (split on whitespace + lowercase, strip punctuation) → words.
   - Computes intersection. If `len(intersection) < min_overlap` AND `parent_template != "general"`, returns `is_refused=True` with reason `"follow-up has zero shared keywords with parent template '{template}'; out-of-scope"`.
   - Otherwise returns `is_refused=False` with overlap details.
3. `def compose_or_refuse(agent: FollowUpAgent, parent: ParentRunContext, follow_up: str) -> ComposedQuery | RefusalDecision`:
   - Calls `detect_out_of_scope(parent.template, follow_up)`.
   - If refused, returns the `RefusalDecision`.
   - Else delegates to `agent.compose(parent, follow_up)`.

### Tests `tests/polaris_graph/followup/test_refusal.py` (NEW, ~80 LOC, 6 tests)

1. `test_refuses_zero_overlap_specific_template` — template `clinical_summary`, follow-up `"Why is the sky blue?"` → refused (no `clinical`/`summary` token in follow-up).
2. `test_accepts_one_keyword_overlap` — template `clinical_summary`, follow-up `"Summarize the secondary outcomes"` → not refused (`summary` shared).
3. `test_general_template_never_refuses` — template `general`, follow-up `"random topic"` → not refused (general explicitly bypasses gate).
4. `test_compose_or_refuse_returns_composed_when_in_scope` — typical in-scope case → returns `ComposedQuery`.
5. `test_compose_or_refuse_returns_refusal_when_out_of_scope` — out-of-scope → returns `RefusalDecision` with `is_refused=True`.
6. `test_adversarial_punctuation_and_case` — follow-up `"What about the SUMMARY?!"` → not refused (punctuation stripped, case lowered).

## Risks for Codex Red-Team

1. **False positive refusals.** A heuristic this simple could refuse valid in-scope follow-ups. Mitigation: refusal threshold is `< 1` (zero overlap). Anyone using a single template-keyword in their follow-up gets through. Acceptable tradeoff for MVP per LOC budget.

2. **Template-name design coupling.** Templates without descriptive `_`-separated keywords (e.g. `t1`) would refuse everything. Mitigation: `general` is explicit bypass. Real templates per repo (`clinical_summary`, etc.) have descriptive names.

3. **§9.4 hygiene.** No `try/except: pass`, no magic numbers (the `min_overlap=1` is a named keyword arg), no `time.sleep`, no TODO.

4. **CHARTER §3 LOC cap.** ~130 LOC net (50 src + 80 test). Under 200.

5. **Adversarial test:** `test_refuses_zero_overlap_specific_template` is the explicit adversarial pass-or-fail named in the issue acceptance.

## Acceptance criteria

1. New `src/polaris_graph/followup/refusal.py` with `detect_out_of_scope` and `compose_or_refuse`.
2. Refusal returns a typed `RefusalDecision` carrying the explanation reason.
3. 6 tests pass (including 1 explicit adversarial).
4. CHARTER §3 LOC cap respected (≤200 net).

**Forced enumeration:** before verdict, write one line per criterion 1-4.
**Completeness check:** list files actually read.

verdict: APPROVE | REQUEST_CHANGES
novel_p0: [...]
continuing_p0: [...]
p1: [...]
p2: [...]
convergence_call: continue | accept_remaining
remaining_blockers_for_execution: [...]
```

APPROVE iff zero NOVEL P0 + zero continuing P0 + zero P1.

  24: def compose_with_inheritance(
  25:     agent: FollowUpAgent,
  26:     parent_contract: EvidenceContract,
  27:     follow_up: str,
  28: ) -> tuple[ComposedQuery, list[SourceSpan]]:
  29:     """Compose a follow-up question that inherits the parent's evidence pool.
  30: 
  31:     Returns the ComposedQuery (with evidence_id list inherited from the
  32:     parent's evidence_pool) and a fresh copy of the parent's source spans
  33:     suitable for direct passthrough to the merger.
  34:     """
  35:     parent = ParentRunContext(
  36:         parent_run_id=parent_contract.run_id,
  37:         template=parent_contract.template,
  38:         parent_question=parent_contract.question,
  39:         known_evidence_ids=[s.evidence_id for s in parent_contract.evidence_pool],
  40:         parent_summary=None,
  41:     )
  42:     composed = agent.compose(parent, follow_up)
  43:     inherited = inherit_evidence_pool(parent_contract)
  44:     return composed, inherited
